Matters Limited to Substance and Accident
Lists five universal matters common to substance and accident and comments on multiplicity and being an effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which universal matters apply only to substance and accident, and how the theologians exclude the Necessary from these categories.

Core Idea
Five universal matters belong to substance and accident alone, never to the Necessary.

In the previous lesson, Sanusi established that some universal matters apply to all three categories (Necessary, substance, accident). Now he turns to those that apply only to two: substance and accident. This distinction is crucial for understanding how theologians reason about created beings without attributing imperfection to Allah. The passage lists five key terms and then addresses two of them in detail, showing why they cannot apply to the Necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the five listed matters
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the five terms: specific potentiality, temporal origination, existence through another, multiplicity, and being an effect.
These are all attributes of contingent beings.
2
Examine the discussion on multiplicity
The author explains that the Necessary has no multiplicity in parts or individuals, and multiplicity of attributes is not counted as multiplicity in the essence itself. Also, the truth is that no individual of substance or accident is simple.
This shows why multiplicity cannot be attributed to the Necessary.
3
Examine the discussion on being an effect
An objection is raised: if the theologians hold that God's existence is additional to His quiddity and is an effect, then being an effect would apply to Him. The author begins a response, but the passage is cut off.
The incomplete response likely argues that the effect is His existence, not His essence.
4
Reflect on the overall principle
These five matters are shared by substance and accident because they are contingent. The Necessary is exempt from them by virtue of His necessity and perfection.

Source Text

أو يشمل الاثنين منها كالإمكان الخاص والحدوث والوجوب بالغير والكثرة والمعلولية فإنها كلها مشتركة بين الجـوهـر والعرض . قوله : ( والكثرة والمعلولية) فإن الواجب أعني ذاته تعالى لا كثرة فيه من حيث الأجزاء ولا من حيث الجزئيات والكثرة باعتبار الصفات ليست كثرة في الواجب، وكذا الحال في المعلولية فإن المعلول على تقدير زيادة الوجود وجوده تعالى لا ذاته المقتضية له فتدبر، فإنه زل فيه الأقدام . قوله : ( والكثرة) أي بحسب الأجزاء أو الجزئيات، وأما كثرة الصفات على القول بها فلا معنى لعدها كثرة في الذات نفسها، ثم الحق أن الكثرة في جميع أفراد الجوهر والعرض مبني على أنه لا يوجد منهما فرد بسيط ذهناً وخارجاً ، ولو أريد بالشمول مجرد وجود الوحدة لا الوجود في كل فرد لم يحتج إلى ذلك البناء. قوله : ( والمعلولية) فإن قلت : عد المعلولية مما يشمل الاثنين فقط لا يستقيم على أصل المتكلمين لأن وجوده تعالى زائد على ماهيته ومعلول لها عندهم فقد تحقق المعلولية في
Or it includes the two (substance and accident) from them, like specific potentiality (al-imkan al-khas), temporal origination (al-huduth), existence through another (al-wujud bi-al-ghayr), multiplicity (al-kathrah), and being an effect (al-ma'luliyyah), for all of these are common between substance and accident. His statement: 'and multiplicity and being an effect' — for the Necessary, I mean His essence, has no multiplicity from the perspective of parts nor from the perspective of individuals, and multiplicity in terms of attributes is not multiplicity in the Necessary. Likewise is the case for being an effect, for the effect, on the assumption of the addition of existence, is His existence, not His essence that necessitates it, so reflect, for feet have slipped therein. His statement: 'and multiplicity' i.e., in terms of parts or individuals. As for multiplicity of attributes, if it is affirmed, there is no meaning to counting it as multiplicity in the essence itself. Then the truth is that multiplicity in all individuals of substance and accident is based on the fact that there does not exist from them a simple individual, mentally and externally. And if by inclusion is meant merely the existence of unity, not existence in every individual, then this basis is not needed. His statement: 'and being an effect' — for if you say: counting being an effect among things that include only two is not sound according to the foundation of the theologians, because His existence is additional to His quiddity and is an effect of it according to them, so being an effect is realized in

Commentary
This passage lists and explains examples of matters that include only two categories of existents: substance (al-jawhar) and accident (al-'arad), but not the Necessary. The examples are: specific potentiality (al-imkan al-khas), temporal origination (al-huduth), existence through another (al-wujud bi-al-ghayr), multiplicity (al-kathrah), and being an effect (al-ma'luliyyah). The author then comments on multiplicity and being an effect in more detail. For multiplicity, he clarifies that the Necessary has no multiplicity in terms of parts or individuals; even multiplicity of attributes does not count as multiplicity in the essence itself. The truth is that multiplicity in all individuals of substance and accident is based on the fact that no individual of substance or accident is simple both mentally and externally. If by 'inclusion' we merely mean the existence of the concept (like unity) rather than existence in every individual, then this foundation is not needed. For being an effect, an objection is raised: on the theologians' view, the existence of the Necessary is additional to His quiddity and is an effect of it, so being an effect would be realized in the Necessary as well, contradicting its limitation to substance and accident. The passage begins to address this but is cut off at the end of the page.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that being an effect applies to the Necessary if one holds that His existence is additional to His quiddity.
Correction: The author suggests that the effect is His existence, not His essence, so being an effect does not apply to the essence of the Necessary.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT listed as an example of a matter that includes only substance and accident?
A) Specific potentiality (al-imkan al-khas)
B) Existence through another (al-wujud bi-al-ghayr)
C) Existence (al-wujud)
D) Multiplicity (al-kathrah)

Key Takeaway
The five universal matters—specific potentiality, temporal origination, existence through another, multiplicity, and being an effect—apply exclusively to created substances and accidents, not to the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever subconsciously imagined Allah as having parts or being limited by multiplicity? What step will you take today to purify your tawhid from such subtle shirk?
?
The passage warns that feet have slipped regarding the notion of God as an effect. Examine your own understanding: do you truly believe that nothing—not even a quiddity—acts upon Allah? How can you guard against this error in your daily thoughts?
?
If all created beings are defined by dependency and contingency, how does that change the way you view your own need for Allah? Will you commit to reciting al-Samad today with full awareness of His absolute independence and your total reliance?

Why Tashakhkhus and Mahiyyah Are Not Universal Matters
Initial argument and refutation of an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why tashakhkhus and mahiyyah are not counted among the universal matters except on the view that they are distinct from the essence.

Core Idea
Individuation and quiddity are parts of external existents, not universal.

The commentator begins by clarifying the status of tashakhkhus and mahiyyah. He explains that these are not universal matters because they are parts of particular existents. This leads to a refutation of a common objection about quiddity applying to the Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once through, focusing on the logical flow from the initial statement to the refutation of the objection.
The passage uses technical terms like imkan khas and huduth.
2
Identify the main claim
Find where the commentator states that tashakhkhus and mahiyyah are not universal except for those who affirm distinctness (al-mughayara).
Look for the phrase 'إلا عند القائل بالتغاير'.
3
Understand the refutation of the objection
The commentator rejects the idea that quiddity as a universal reality applies to the Necessary. He distinguishes between two meanings of quiddity.
One meaning is logical 'what is it?', the other is the real quiddity making a thing what it is.
4
Connect to the learning objective
Ask yourself: why does this distinction matter for the study of universal matters in kalam?
This prepares you for seeing how tashakhkhus differs from existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لوجوده وتشخص مغاير لماهيته أو يشمل الاثنين منها كالإمكان الخاص والحدوث والوجوب بالغير والكثرة والمعلولية فإنها كلها مشتركة بين الجـوهـر والعرض

التشخص جزء من الشخص الموجود في الخارج وجزء الموجود موجود فلا يكونان من الأمور الشاملة للثلاثة إلا عند القائل بالتغاير فاندفع البحث المشهور من أن الماهية والتشخص يصدقان على الواجب سواء كان وجوده وتشخصه عين ماهيته أو غيرها، وأما ما قيل: الجواب أن الماهية تطلق على الحقيقة الكلية ولذا قيل : الماهية تدل على الكلية التزاما وهي من في الواجب، فليس بشيء لأن ذلك في الماهية بمعنى ما به يجاب عن السؤال بما هو وهو مصطلح المنطقيين دون بمعنى ما به الشيء هو هو المبحوث عنه في الأمور العامة كيف ولو كان كذلك لما صح قولهم تشخص الواجب ووجوده عين ماهيته.

وهي منتفية

قوله : ( والكثرة والمعلولية) فإن الواجب أعني ذاته تعالى لا كثرة فيه من حيث الأجزاء ولا من حيث الجزئيات والكثرة باعتبار الصفات ليست كثرة في الواجب، وكذا الحال في المعلولية فإن المعلول على تقدير زيادة الوجود وجوده تعالى لا ذاته المقتضية له فتدبر، فإنه زل فيه الأقدام .

الكلام في الماهية سواء كانت عين وجوده أو لا وقد يقرر الاعتراض بعبارة أخرى، وهي أن القائل بأن ماهية الواجب تعالى عين وجوده وتشخصه هو الفلاسفة، وهم لا يقولون بعينية الماهية المطلقة والتشخص المطلق اللذين هما من الأمور العامة، بل بزيادتهما، ويمكن أن يؤيد الاعتراض بأن الشارح عد الماهية في حواشي التجريد من الشاملة للكل كالوجود والجواب أن الماهية تطلق غالباً على الحقيقة الكلية، ومن ثمة قيل لفظ الماهية يدل على الكلية التزاماً، والتشخص عندهم هو المنضم إلى الماهية ، ولذا صرحوا بأن التشخص غير الماهية، واستدلوا على ذلك وحكم المحققون بوجودية التشخص واستدلوا بجزئيته من المعين الموجود في الخارج فعلى تقدير كون تشخص الباري عز اسمه عين ماهيته وماهيته تعالى عين ،وجوده لم يوجد فيه ما يصدق عليه مفهوم الماهية المطلقة، والتشخص المطلق اللذين هما من الأمور العامة؛ وهذا ظاهر وما ذكره في حواشي التجريد مبني على إرادة الحقيقة من الماهية والحق أن الفرق بين الوجود والتشخص في كون الأول من الشاملة للثلاثة ولو قيل : بكون وجود الباري تعالى عينه كما يدل عليه عدم التقييد وعدم كون الثاني إلا على تقدير المغايرة خفي وسيرد عليك ما يفيدك بصيرة في المقام . قوله : ( وتشخص مغاير لماهيته لكنه غير داخل في هويته إذ لا قائل بالتركيب فلا ينافي هناك نفي الكثرة .
By his existence and individuation distinct from his quiddity or includes both, such as specific possibility, temporal origination, existence through another, multiplicity, and being effect; for all are common between substance and accident. Individuation is a part of the individual existing in the external, and a part of the existent is existent; so they are not among the matters inclusive of the three except for the one who affirms distinctness. Thus the famous inquiry is repelled: that quiddity and individuation are predicated of the Necessary, whether his existence and individuation are identical to his quiddity or not. As for what was said: 'The answer is that quiddity is applied to the universal reality, hence it was said: quiddity indicates being universal by entailment, and it is present in the Necessary,' it is nothing, because that is quiddity in the sense of that by which one answers the question 'what is it?', which is the terminology of the logicians, not in the sense of that by which a thing is what it is, which is the subject of inquiry in the universal matters. How could it be? For if it were so, their statement 'the individuation of the Necessary and his existence are identical to his quiddity' would not be valid. And it is negated. His saying: (Multiplicity and being effect) – for the Necessary, I mean His essence, may He be exalted, has no multiplicity in terms of parts nor in terms of instances; multiplicity with respect to attributes is not multiplicity in the Necessary. The same applies to being effect, for the effect, on the assumption of the superaddition of existence, is His existence, may He be exalted, not His essence which necessitates it. So reflect, for in it the feet have slipped. The discussion concerns quiddity whether it is identical to His existence or not. The objection may be formulated in another expression: that the one who says that the quiddity of the Necessary, may He be exalted, is identical to His existence and individuation are the philosophers, and they do not affirm the identity of absolute quiddity and absolute individuation, which are among the universal matters, but rather their superaddition. The objection may be supported by the fact that the commentator counted quiddity in the margins of al-Tajrid among those inclusive of all, like existence. The answer is that quiddity is usually applied to the universal reality, and hence it was said that the word quiddity indicates universality by entailment; individuation for them is what is attached to quiddity, and thus they stated that individuation is other than quiddity, and they argued for that. The verifiers judged that individuation is existential and argued for its particularity from the specific existent in the external. So on the assumption that the individuation of the Creator, may His name be exalted, is identical to His quiddity, and His quiddity, may He be exalted, is identical to His existence, there is found in Him nothing to which the concept of absolute quiddity and absolute individuation, which are among the universal matters, applies. This is apparent. What he mentioned in the margins of al-Tajrid is based on intending reality from quiddity. The truth is that the difference between existence and individuation lies in the fact that the first is among the inclusive of the three, even if it is said: that the existence of the Creator, may He be exalted, is identical to Him, as indicated by the lack of restriction, and that the second is not except on the assumption of distinctness, is subtle, and what will come to you will give you insight into the matter. His saying: (And individuation distinct from his quiddity but not included in his identity, for no one affirms composition, so it does not contradict the negation of multiplicity.)
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the author's listing of universal matters, focusing on whether tashakhkhus (individuation) and mahiyyah (quiddity) are universal matters. The commentator begins by restating the author's claim that individuation is either distinct from quiddity or includes both, and then lists examples like specific possibility, temporal origination, existence through another, multiplicity, and being effect, all common between substance and accident. The commentator then explains that individuation is a part of an external individual, and a part of the existent must itself be existent; hence individuation and quiddity are not universal matters (i.e., not common to the Necessary, substance, and accident) except for those who hold that they are distinct from the essence (i.e., that quiddity and individuation are accidents added to the essence). This refutes the common objection that quiddity and individuation apply to the Necessary regardless of whether they are identical to His existence or not. The commentator then addresses an objection: it was said that quiddity is a universal reality, so it applies to the Necessary. The commentator rejects this, noting that "quiddity" here is used in the logical sense (as the answer to 'what is it?'), not in the sense of 'what makes a thing what it is,' which is the subject of universal matters. If it were the latter, then the philosophers' statement that the Necessary's individuation and existence are identical to His quiddity would be impossible. After a short remark ('it is negated'), the commentator glosses the author's mention of 'multiplicity and being effect.' Multiplicity in the Necessary is not in terms of parts or instances; multiplicity of attributes is not multiplicity in the essence. Similarly, 'being effect' applies only to the existence of the Necessary (on the view of superaddition), not to His essence. Then the commentator returns to the discussion of quiddity, recasting the objection: the philosophers affirm that the Necessary's quiddity is identical to His existence and individuation, but they do not affirm identity of *absolute* quiddity and *absolute* individuation (which are universal); rather they hold these to be superadded. The objection is supported by the commentator's own statement in his marginal notes on al-Tajrid that quiddity is among the universal matters. The answer is that 'quiddity' usually means the universal reality, and individuation is what attaches to it, so the philosophers themselves say individuation is other than quiddity. Thus, even if the Necessary's individuation is identical to His quiddity, the concept of absolute quiddity does not apply. The commentator concludes that the truth is that existence is among the three (Necessary, substance, accident) while individuation is not, except on the view of distinctness. He ends with a brief gloss affirming that individuation is distinct from quiddity but not part of the identity, so it does not imply composition.

Key Terms
tashakhkhus (تشخص)
mahiyyah (ماهية)
umur 'ammah (أمور عامة)
imkan khas (إمكان خاص)
huduth (حدوث)
wujub bil-ghayr (وجوب بالغير)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that quiddity as a universal concept applies to the Necessary in the same way as to created beings.
Correction: The passage explains that the relevant meaning of quiddity is not the logical universal but the real quiddity that makes a thing what it is; this does not apply to the Necessary in a universal sense.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why are tashakhkhus and mahiyyah not universal matters?
A) Because they are not common to substance and accident.
B) Because individuation is part of an external individual and a part of the existent is existent, so they are not found in the Necessary Being except on the view of distinctness.
C) Because the philosophers say they are identical to the Necessary's essence.
D) Because they are only found in accidents.

Key Takeaway
Tashakhkhus and mahiyyah are not universal matters because they are parts of particular existents, not common concepts, except on the view of distinctness where they become accidental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you tend to label people and things by their universal categories (e.g., 'student', 'friend') rather than recognizing their unique essence as created individuals before Allah? How does this lesson's insistence on the non-universality of real quiddity call you to see others as distinct souls rather than mere concepts? What one step can you take today to see someone as their real quiddity, not just a label?
?
The passage distinguishes between the logical sense of quiddity (answering 'what is it?') and the real sense (that by which a thing is what it is). When you reflect on your own existence, do you ever confuse your role or identity (logical quiddity) with your true self before Allah? What would it mean for you to live according to your real quiddity as a servant of Allah, rather than according to worldly definitions?
?
The Necessary Being's tashakhkhus and mahiyyah are identical to His existence, unlike contingent beings. How does this truth affect your understanding of Allah's absolute uniqueness? Are there moments when you inadvertently imagine Allah through the lens of created quiddities? How can you actively guard your heart from that misconception in worship?

Distinguishing Existence and Individuation in Universality
Commentary on multiplicity, being effect, and final distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how existence is a universal matter while individuation is not, and how the philosophers' view on tashakhkhus and mahiyyah supports this distinction.

Core Idea
Existence is universal; individuation is not, because it is particular even in the Necessary.

The commentator continues by explaining why multiplicity and being effect do not apply to the Necessary, then addresses the philosophers' position on quiddity and individuation. He clarifies that absolute quiddity and absolute individuation are not identical to the Necessary's essence, which is why they are not universal matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the second part of the commentary, focusing on the glosses and the recast objection.
Pay attention to the distinction between existence and individuation.
2
Understand the gloss on multiplicity and being effect
The commentator explains that the Necessary has no multiplicity in parts or instances; multiplicity of attributes is not real multiplicity. Similarly, 'being effect' applies only to existence, not essence.
These examples show how some universal matters do not apply to the Necessary.
3
Follow the recast objection and its answer
The objection: the philosophers deny that absolute quiddity is identical to the Necessary's essence, so it cannot be a universal matter. The answer: 'quiddity' usually means the universal reality, and individuation is attached to it, so the philosophers' view that individuation is other than quiddity implies that absolute quiddity and absolute individuation are not present in the Necessary.
This reinforces the initial point.
4
Grasp the final distinction
The commentator concludes that existence is among the three (Necessary, substance, accident) while individuation is not, except on the view of distinctness.
This is a key takeaway for the whole discussion.

لوجوده وتشخص مغاير لماهيته أو يشمل الاثنين منها كالإمكان الخاص والحدوث والوجوب بالغير والكثرة والمعلولية فإنها كلها مشتركة بين الجـوهـر والعرض

التشخص جزء من الشخص الموجود في الخارج وجزء الموجود موجود فلا يكونان من الأمور الشاملة للثلاثة إلا عند القائل بالتغاير فاندفع البحث المشهور من أن الماهية والتشخص يصدقان على الواجب سواء كان وجوده وتشخصه عين ماهيته أو غيرها، وأما ما قيل: الجواب أن الماهية تطلق على الحقيقة الكلية ولذا قيل : الماهية تدل على الكلية التزاما وهي من في الواجب، فليس بشيء لأن ذلك في الماهية بمعنى ما به يجاب عن السؤال بما هو وهو مصطلح المنطقيين دون بمعنى ما به الشيء هو هو المبحوث عنه في الأمور العامة كيف ولو كان كذلك لما صح قولهم تشخص الواجب ووجوده عين ماهيته.

وهي منتفية

قوله : ( والكثرة والمعلولية) فإن الواجب أعني ذاته تعالى لا كثرة فيه من حيث الأجزاء ولا من حيث الجزئيات والكثرة باعتبار الصفات ليست كثرة في الواجب، وكذا الحال في المعلولية فإن المعلول على تقدير زيادة الوجود وجوده تعالى لا ذاته المقتضية له فتدبر، فإنه زل فيه الأقدام .

الكلام في الماهية سواء كانت عين وجوده أو لا وقد يقرر الاعتراض بعبارة أخرى، وهي أن القائل بأن ماهية الواجب تعالى عين وجوده وتشخصه هو الفلاسفة، وهم لا يقولون بعينية الماهية المطلقة والتشخص المطلق اللذين هما من الأمور العامة، بل بزيادتهما، ويمكن أن يؤيد الاعتراض بأن الشارح عد الماهية في حواشي التجريد من الشاملة للكل كالوجود والجواب أن الماهية تطلق غالباً على الحقيقة الكلية، ومن ثمة قيل لفظ الماهية يدل على الكلية التزاماً، والتشخص عندهم هو المنضم إلى الماهية ، ولذا صرحوا بأن التشخص غير الماهية، واستدلوا على ذلك وحكم المحققون بوجودية التشخص واستدلوا بجزئيته من المعين الموجود في الخارج فعلى تقدير كون تشخص الباري عز اسمه عين ماهيته وماهيته تعالى عين ،وجوده لم يوجد فيه ما يصدق عليه مفهوم الماهية المطلقة، والتشخص المطلق اللذين هما من الأمور العامة؛ وهذا ظاهر وما ذكره في حواشي التجريد مبني على إرادة الحقيقة من الماهية والحق أن الفرق بين الوجود والتشخص في كون الأول من الشاملة للثلاثة ولو قيل : بكون وجود الباري تعالى عينه كما يدل عليه عدم التقييد وعدم كون الثاني إلا على تقدير المغايرة خفي وسيرد عليك ما يفيدك بصيرة في المقام . قوله : ( وتشخص مغاير لماهيته لكنه غير داخل في هويته إذ لا قائل بالتركيب فلا ينافي هناك نفي الكثرة .
By his existence and individuation distinct from his quiddity or includes both, such as specific possibility, temporal origination, existence through another, multiplicity, and being effect; for all are common between substance and accident. Individuation is a part of the individual existing in the external, and a part of the existent is existent; so they are not among the matters inclusive of the three except for the one who affirms distinctness. Thus the famous inquiry is repelled: that quiddity and individuation are predicated of the Necessary, whether his existence and individuation are identical to his quiddity or not. As for what was said: 'The answer is that quiddity is applied to the universal reality, hence it was said: quiddity indicates being universal by entailment, and it is present in the Necessary,' it is nothing, because that is quiddity in the sense of that by which one answers the question 'what is it?', which is the terminology of the logicians, not in the sense of that by which a thing is what it is, which is the subject of inquiry in the universal matters. How could it be? For if it were so, their statement 'the individuation of the Necessary and his existence are identical to his quiddity' would not be valid. And it is negated. His saying: (Multiplicity and being effect) – for the Necessary, I mean His essence, may He be exalted, has no multiplicity in terms of parts nor in terms of instances; multiplicity with respect to attributes is not multiplicity in the Necessary. The same applies to being effect, for the effect, on the assumption of the superaddition of existence, is His existence, may He be exalted, not His essence which necessitates it. So reflect, for in it the feet have slipped. The discussion concerns quiddity whether it is identical to His existence or not. The objection may be formulated in another expression: that the one who says that the quiddity of the Necessary, may He be exalted, is identical to His existence and individuation are the philosophers, and they do not affirm the identity of absolute quiddity and absolute individuation, which are among the universal matters, but rather their superaddition. The objection may be supported by the fact that the commentator counted quiddity in the margins of al-Tajrid among those inclusive of all, like existence. The answer is that quiddity is usually applied to the universal reality, and hence it was said that the word quiddity indicates universality by entailment; individuation for them is what is attached to quiddity, and thus they stated that individuation is other than quiddity, and they argued for that. The verifiers judged that individuation is existential and argued for its particularity from the specific existent in the external. So on the assumption that the individuation of the Creator, may His name be exalted, is identical to His quiddity, and His quiddity, may He be exalted, is identical to His existence, there is found in Him nothing to which the concept of absolute quiddity and absolute individuation, which are among the universal matters, applies. This is apparent. What he mentioned in the margins of al-Tajrid is based on intending reality from quiddity. The truth is that the difference between existence and individuation lies in the fact that the first is among the inclusive of the three, even if it is said: that the existence of the Creator, may He be exalted, is identical to Him, as indicated by the lack of restriction, and that the second is not except on the assumption of distinctness, is subtle, and what will come to you will give you insight into the matter. His saying: (And individuation distinct from his quiddity but not included in his identity, for no one affirms composition, so it does not contradict the negation of multiplicity.)
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the author's listing of universal matters, focusing on whether tashakhkhus (individuation) and mahiyyah (quiddity) are universal matters. The commentator begins by restating the author's claim that individuation is either distinct from quiddity or includes both, and then lists examples like specific possibility, temporal origination, existence through another, multiplicity, and being effect, all common between substance and accident. The commentator then explains that individuation is a part of an external individual, and a part of the existent must itself be existent; hence individuation and quiddity are not universal matters (i.e., not common to the Necessary, substance, and accident) except for those who hold that they are distinct from the essence (i.e., that quiddity and individuation are accidents added to the essence). This refutes the common objection that quiddity and individuation apply to the Necessary regardless of whether they are identical to His existence or not. The commentator then addresses an objection: it was said that quiddity is a universal reality, so it applies to the Necessary. The commentator rejects this, noting that "quiddity" here is used in the logical sense (as the answer to 'what is it?'), not in the sense of 'what makes a thing what it is,' which is the subject of universal matters. If it were the latter, then the philosophers' statement that the Necessary's individuation and existence are identical to His quiddity would be impossible. After a short remark ('it is negated'), the commentator glosses the author's mention of 'multiplicity and being effect.' Multiplicity in the Necessary is not in terms of parts or instances; multiplicity of attributes is not multiplicity in the essence. Similarly, 'being effect' applies only to the existence of the Necessary (on the view of superaddition), not to His essence. Then the commentator returns to the discussion of quiddity, recasting the objection: the philosophers affirm that the Necessary's quiddity is identical to His existence and individuation, but they do not affirm identity of *absolute* quiddity and *absolute* individuation (which are universal); rather they hold these to be superadded. The objection is supported by the commentator's own statement in his marginal notes on al-Tajrid that quiddity is among the universal matters. The answer is that 'quiddity' usually means the universal reality, and individuation is what attaches to it, so the philosophers themselves say individuation is other than quiddity. Thus, even if the Necessary's individuation is identical to His quiddity, the concept of absolute quiddity does not apply. The commentator concludes that the truth is that existence is among the three (Necessary, substance, accident) while individuation is not, except on the view of distinctness. He ends with a brief gloss affirming that individuation is distinct from quiddity but not part of the identity, so it does not imply composition.

Key Terms
kathrah (كثرة)
ma'luliyyah (معلولية)
wujud (وجود)
tashakhkhus (تشخص)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that individuation is a universal matter like existence.
Correction: The passage argues that existence is universal while individuation is not, because individuation is particular to each existent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is individuation not a universal matter while existence is?
A) Because existence is common to all three categories, but individuation is only in individuals.
B) Because individuation is not found in the Necessary.
C) Because the philosophers say so.
D) Because existence is an accident.

Key Takeaway
Existence is a universal matter predicated of the Necessary, substance, and accident, while individuation is not, because it is particular to each existent and only becomes universal on the view of distinctness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own existence is separate from your essence—you are a contingent being who could have been otherwise. Does this truth make you feel your absolute dependence on the Necessary One every moment? Will you today consciously turn to Him as the One who gives you existence and individuation?
?
The passage shows that only in the Necessary are quiddity and existence identical. You, however, are a composite of essence and existence. How does this fundamental difference between you and your Creator reshape your understanding of worship? Will you let this difference humble you in your prayers today?
?
Individuation for you is a gift from Allah—it makes you a unique person, but it is not universal. Are you content with being a particular, limited being, or do you secretly aspire to transcend your individuality as if you were self-sufficient? Examine your heart: do you accept your creaturely limitations or do you resent them?

Why Nonexistence and Similar Concepts Are Not Universal Matters
Commentator argues against universality of four attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why nonexistence, impossibility, essential necessity, and eternity are not considered universal matters in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Not every broad concept is a universal matter; these four lack actual instantiation in existents.

The commentator argues that certain key terms, though often discussed, do not qualify as 'universal matters' (umur 'ammah) because they are not actually found in the three categories of existents: the Necessary, substance, and accident. This passage clarifies the definitions and shows why the discussion of these terms here is subsidiary, not central.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the translation. Notice the commentator's style of glossing the author's words.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فعلى هذا' which sets up the objection.
2
Identify the four concepts and the claim
The commentator lists 'adam, imtina', wujub dhati, and qidam. He claims they are not universal matters. Underline why he says this.
3
Examine the argument for nonexistence and impossibility
The commentator explains that they are not found in any of the three categories. For the Necessary, it's obvious. For substance and accident, he contrasts theological and philosophical definitions.
Note that even philosophical definitions imply existence, so nonexistence cannot be a universal matter.
4
Understand the point about eternity
Eternity (qidam) means 'lack of being preceded by nonexistence.' This is specific to the Necessary and not found in substance or accident.
Eternal attributes are also excluded because they are not part of the definitions of substance and accident.
5
Reflect on the objection to the author
The commentator notes that if these are not universal matters, then discussing them here is digressive, which conflicts with the author's stated subject.
This shows the importance of the discussion for the work's coherence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلى هذا لا يكون العدم والامتناع والوجوب الذاتي والقدم من الأمور العامة، ويكون البحث عنها هاهنا على سبيل التبعية، وقد يقال الأمور العامة ما يتناول المفهومات بأسرها إما

قوله : (فعلى هذا إلخ) بيان للواقع واعتراض على المصنف بأنه يلزم أن يكون البحث عنها استطرادياً عنده، وهو لا يناسب جعله الموضوع المعلوم من حيث يتعلق به إثبات العقائد الدينية والأمور المذكورة داخلة فيه.

قوله : ( لا يكون العدم إلخ ) إذ لا يوجدان في شيء منهما فضلاً عن الشمول أما عن الشمول أما في الواجب فظاهر وأما في الجوهر والعرض فلأنهما عند المتكلمين عبارتان عن الحادث المتحيز بالذات، وعن الحادث القائم بالمتحيز بالذات على ما يجيء وما قيل من أن الجوهر عبارة عن ماهية إذا وجدت كانت لا في موضوع والعرض عن ماهية إذا وجدت كانت في موضوع، فلا يكون الوجود معتبراً فيهما بالفعل، فيشملهما العدم ففيه أنه من مصطلحات الفلاسفة على أنه نص الشيخ بأن الوجود بالفعل معتبر في العرض عندهم، والتعميم في الجوهر لإدخال صور الجوهر لا لأن الوجود ليس بمعتبر فيه كيف وقد قسموا الموجود الممكن إلى الجوهر والعرض كما سيجيء .

قوله : ( والقدم) بمعنى عدم المسبوقية بالعدم فإنه مختص بالواجب لا يوجد في الجوهر والعرض والصفات القديمة خارجة عنهما لما عرفت من تعريفهما .

قوله : ( المفهومات ( أي الواجب والممتنع والممكن.
Based on this, nonexistence, impossibility, essential necessity, and eternity are not among the universal matters, and the discussion of them here is by way of being subsidiary. It may be said that universal matters are what encompass the concepts entirely, either... His saying: (Based on this, etc.) is an explanation of the reality and an objection to the author that it follows that the discussion of them is digressive in his view, and that does not suit his making the known subject [of this work] insofar as it pertains to establishing religious beliefs, while the mentioned matters are included in it. His saying: (Nonexistence, etc.) because they are not found in any of the two [the Necessary and the contingent], let alone being inclusive. As for inclusion: as for the Necessary, it is apparent; as for substance and accident, because for the theologians they are expressions for the temporal event that is self-subsisting in place, and for the temporal event that subsists in what is self-subsisting in place, as will come. And what was said—that substance is a quiddity which, when it exists, is not in a subject, and accident is a quiddity which, when it exists, is in a subject—so existence is not considered in them actually; thus nonexistence encompasses them—that [response] is from the terminology of the philosophers, although the principal (al-Shaykh) stated that actual existence is considered in accident for them, and the generalization in substance is to include forms of substance, not because existence is not considered in it. How could it be, since they divided the possible existent into substance and accident, as will come? His saying: (And eternity) meaning lack of being preceded by nonexistence; it is specific to the Necessary, not found in substance and accident, and the eternal attributes are outside them due to what you have known of their definition. His saying: (The concepts) i.e., the Necessary, the impossible, and the possible.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of why nonexistence ('adam), impossibility (imtina'), essential necessity (wujub dhati), and eternity (qidam) are not considered universal matters (al-umur al-'ammah). The commentator begins by stating that based on the previous discussion, these four are not universal; they are only discussed here subsidiary. He then provides a gloss on the author's phrase 'based on this,' noting that it is both an explanation and an objection: if these are not universal, then discussing them here would be digressive, which does not suit the stated subject of the work (establishing religious beliefs). Then he explains why nonexistence and impossibility are not universal: they are not found in any of the three categories (Necessary, substance, accident). For the Necessary, it is obvious; for substance and accident, the commentator notes two definitions. According to the theologians, substance is a temporal event that is self-subsisting in place, and accident is a temporal event that subsists in a place; these definitions already include existence, so nonexistence does not apply to them. If one uses the philosophical definitions (substance is a quiddity that when existent is not in a subject; accident is a quiddity that when existent is in a subject), then existence is not considered actual in these definitions, so nonexistence could encompass them. But the commentator says that is philosophical terminology; the philosophers (al-Shaykh) actually consider existence as actual in accident, and in substance they include forms (which are not actual? Actually, they divide possible existent into substance and accident, implying existence). So the philosophical definition also implicitly assumes existence. Thus nonexistence is not a universal matter. As for eternity (qidam), meaning lack of being preceded by nonexistence, it is specific to the Necessary and not found in substance or accident; eternal attributes are also excluded because they are not included in the definitions of substance and accident. The passage ends with a gloss defining 'the concepts' as the Necessary, the impossible, and the possible.

امتناع
وجوب ذاتي
قدم
أمور عامة

A) Because it is not found in the Necessary Being.
B) Because it is not found in substance and accident as defined by the theologians.
C) Because even in philosophical definitions, existence is implied.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
Nonexistence, impossibility, essential necessity, and eternity are not universal matters because they are not actually instantiated in the three categories of existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often think about what is 'possible' and 'impossible' in your life. But what if, like the concept of impossibility itself, many of those limitations are not actual in external reality? Which 'impossibility' are you treating as a universal barrier that this lesson calls you to reexamine?
?
Eternity is an attribute of God alone. Do you truly live as if only God is eternal, or do you sometimes act as if this world or your own plans will last forever? Where has your heart attached to something that is not truly eternal, and what would it mean to reorient that attachment today?
?
The commentator argues that even our definitions must align with reality—theologians define substance and accident as temporal events that exist. Are there concepts you hold about God or yourself that you have never tested against what is actually true? Will you sit with this lesson and let it challenge one such concept right now?

Clarification on the Necessary and Being an Effect
Commentator clarifies the concept of being an effect in relation to the Necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why being an effect (ma'luliyyah) cannot be attributed to the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
The Necessary cannot be an effect because need for a cause arises only from temporal origination or possibility.

The commentator begins by addressing a subtle point about the Necessary Being and the concept of 'being an effect'. This clarification is essential for understanding the later discussion of universal matters. Before diving into the main argument, he refines what is meant by the Necessary's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense style of the commentary.
2
Identify the key claim about ma'luliyyah
The passage asserts that being an effect is absolutely not conceivable in the Necessary. Find the reasoning provided.
3
Understand the reasoning
The cause of need for a cause is either temporal origination or possibility. Since the Necessary has neither, it cannot be an effect. Even the superadded existence is possible in itself, not the essence.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الواجب تعالى بمعنى أن وجوده من العلة، قلت بعد تسليم أن ليس المراد المعلولية للغير لا تعقل المعلولية في الواجب قطعاً لأن علة الاحتياج إلى العلة إما الحدوث أو الإمكان والمعلول على تقدير زيادة وجوده ،تعالى هو الاتصاف بالوجود الذي هو ممكن بالنظر إلى نفسه لا نفس ذاته الذي هو الواجب، وسيصرح الشارح بهذا في المقصد الثالث في هذا الموقف، فبناء الجواب الثاني عليه لكن الكلام بعد على هذا الجواب محل تأمل، فتأمل. يمنع ذلك

، قوله: (فعلى هذا لا يكون العدم والامتناع والوجوب الذاتي والقدم من الأمور العامة قد في العدم ويدعى أنه من الأحوال المشتركة بين الاثنين أعني الجوهر والعرض، إذ المراد بالجوهر ماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت لا في موضوع وكذا المراد بالعرض ماهية لو وجدت في الخارج كانت في موضوع ويؤيد ذلك أن المصنف عنون الفصل الأول بالوجود والعدم معا وأنت خبير بأن الشيء إذا سلم اتصافه بالجوهرية أو العرضية حال العدم لم يكن الجوهر المطلق ولا العرض المطلق من أقسام الموجود الخارجي بناء على ما هو الحق من وجوب كون القسم أخص من المقسم مطلقا، بل قسمة ما هو الموجد منهما فلا يكون العدم من الأمور العامة قطعا، إذ ليس يتحقق في موجود خارجي أصلا فضلا عن أن يوجد في أكثر من قسم منه وأما القدم فإن أريد به القدم الذاتي فظاهر أنه ليس من الأمور العامة وإن أريد عدم المسبوقية بالعدم فعدم عده منها مبني على أن الصفات ليست من الأعراض، إذ لو عدت منها وأريد بها هاهنا ما لا يقوم بنفسها كما ينبئ عنه حصرهم أقسام الموجود في الثلاثة سيما على القول ببقاء الأعراض كما يقول : بعض المتكلمين كان القدم المطلق من الأمور العامة بالتفسير المذكور. قوله : (ما يتناول المفهومات بأسرها وهذا هو المناسب بمعنى العموم وعموم الموضوع للموجود والمعدوم على ما اختاره المصنف
The Necessary, may He be exalted, meaning that His existence is from the cause. I say: after conceding that what is meant is not being effect for another, being effect is absolutely not conceivable in the Necessary, because the cause of need for a cause is either temporal origination or possibility, and the effect, on the assumption of the superaddition of His existence, is the qualification by existence which is possible in itself, not the self itself which is the Necessary. The commentator will explicitly state this in the third section of this position. So building the second answer upon it, but the discussion regarding this answer is a place for reflection, so reflect. That is prevented. Then his saying: (Based on this, nonexistence, impossibility, essential necessity, and eternity are not among the universal matters) – regarding nonexistence, it is claimed that it is among the common states between the two, namely substance and accident, because what is meant by substance is a quiddity which, if it existed in the external, would be not in a subject, and likewise what is meant by accident is a quiddity which, if it existed in the external, would be in a subject. That is supported by the fact that the author entitled the first chapter 'Existence and Nonexistence' together. And you are aware that if a thing is conceded to be qualified by substantiality or accidentalness in the state of nonexistence, then absolute substance and absolute accident would not be divisions of the external existent, based on the truth that the division must be more specific than the divided absolutely. Rather, the division is of what exists from them. Thus nonexistence is certainly not among the universal matters, since it is not realized in any external existent at all, let alone being present in more than one division of it. As for eternity, if what is meant by it is essential eternity, then it is obvious that it is not among the universal matters. If what is meant is lack of being preceded by nonexistence, then its not being counted among them is based on the fact that the attributes are not accidents; for if they were counted among them and what is meant here by them is what does not subsist by itself, as their limiting the divisions of the existent to three indicates, especially on the view of the persistence of accidents as some theologians hold, then absolute eternity would be among the universal matters according to the mentioned interpretation. His saying: (What encompasses the concepts entirely) and this is what is appropriate, meaning the universality and the universality of the subject for both the existent and the nonexistent, according to what the author chose.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of universal matters, beginning with a clarification about the Necessary Being and the concept of 'being an effect' (ma'luliyyah). The commentator notes that if by 'the Necessary' we mean that His existence is from a cause, then after conceding that it is not being an effect for another, being an effect is absolutely not conceivable in the Necessary because need for a cause is due to temporal origination or possibility; the effect in the case of superaddition of existence is the qualification by existence, not the essence itself. This point will be elaborated later. The commentator then returns to the claim that nonexistence, impossibility, essential necessity, and eternity are not universal matters. He addresses an objection that nonexistence might be a common state between substance and accident, based on the definitions of substance and accident as quiddities that would be not in a subject or in a subject if they existed. The objector points out that the author entitled the first chapter 'Existence and Nonexistence' together, implying nonexistence is a common consideration. The commentator refutes this: if a thing is described as substance or accident in the state of nonexistence, then absolute substance and accident would not be divisions of the external existent (since divisions must be more specific than the divided). Actually, the division is of what exists, so nonexistence is not realized in any external existent, let alone being found in more than one division. Then he discusses eternity: if essential eternity (qidam dhati) is meant, it is clearly not universal. If lack of being preceded by nonexistence is meant, its not being universal depends on whether attributes are considered accidents. If attributes are considered accidents and what is meant by 'accident' here is what does not subsist by itself (as indicated by limiting divisions of the existent to three), then on the view that accidents persist, absolute eternity (meaning lack of being preceded by nonexistence) could be universal. The passage ends with a definition: universal matters are what encompass all concepts (al-mafhumat), i.e., the Necessary, the possible, and the impossible. This is appropriate, meaning the subject is universal for both the existent and the nonexistent, as the author chose.

Key Terms
wajib (واجب)
ma'luliyyah (معلولية)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Necessary Being could be considered an effect in some sense because His existence is 'from' His essence.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that being an effect (ma'luliyyah) requires need for a cause, which only applies to originated or possible beings. The Necessary's existence is not caused; even the concept of superadded existence is possible in itself, but the essence is necessary.

Checkpoint
Why is being an effect inconceivable in the Necessary Being?
A) Because the Necessary has no existence.
B) Because need for a cause arises only from temporal origination or possibility, and the Necessary has neither.
C) Because the Necessary is an effect of itself.
D) Because the commentator says so without reason.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being cannot be an effect because need for a cause only arises from temporal origination or possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Necessary has absolutely no cause, why do you still act as if your existence depends on anything other than Him? What does that reveal about your tawheed?
?
The commentator says 'the discussion is a place for reflection.' What have you reflected about your own need for causes? Are you more attached to secondary causes than to the Causer of all?
?
Since you are possible (mumkin), you are in constant need of a cause. How does this truth change the way you handle your daily anxieties and reliance on creation?

Why Nonexistence and Impossibility Are Not Universal Matters
Commentator refutes the idea that nonexistence is a universal matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that nonexistence and impossibility are not among the universal matters is defended against an objection.

Core Idea
Nonexistence cannot be a universal matter because it has no existence in external reality.

The commentator now addresses a specific claim: that nonexistence, impossibility, essential necessity, and eternity are not universal matters. He focuses first on nonexistence, responding to an objection that it might be a common state between substance and accident. This section sharpens our understanding of what qualifies as a universal matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that this passage quotes the author's claim.
2
Identify the objection about nonexistence
The objector claims that nonexistence is a common state between substance and accident. Find the two arguments given in support of this.
One is based on definitions, the other on the chapter title.
3
Understand the refutation
The commentator argues that if substance and accident were attributed to nonexistents, they would not be divisions of external existents. Since a division must be more specific than the divided, substance and accident must be divisions of what exists.
4
Conclude why nonexistence is not universal
Nonexistence is not realized in any external existent, so it cannot be a universal matter (which must be found in more than one division of the existent).
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الواجب تعالى بمعنى أن وجوده من العلة، قلت بعد تسليم أن ليس المراد المعلولية للغير لا تعقل المعلولية في الواجب قطعاً لأن علة الاحتياج إلى العلة إما الحدوث أو الإمكان والمعلول على تقدير زيادة وجوده ،تعالى هو الاتصاف بالوجود الذي هو ممكن بالنظر إلى نفسه لا نفس ذاته الذي هو الواجب، وسيصرح الشارح بهذا في المقصد الثالث في هذا الموقف، فبناء الجواب الثاني عليه لكن الكلام بعد على هذا الجواب محل تأمل، فتأمل. يمنع ذلك

، قوله: (فعلى هذا لا يكون العدم والامتناع والوجوب الذاتي والقدم من الأمور العامة قد في العدم ويدعى أنه من الأحوال المشتركة بين الاثنين أعني الجوهر والعرض، إذ المراد بالجوهر ماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت لا في موضوع وكذا المراد بالعرض ماهية لو وجدت في الخارج كانت في موضوع ويؤيد ذلك أن المصنف عنون الفصل الأول بالوجود والعدم معا وأنت خبير بأن الشيء إذا سلم اتصافه بالجوهرية أو العرضية حال العدم لم يكن الجوهر المطلق ولا العرض المطلق من أقسام الموجود الخارجي بناء على ما هو الحق من وجوب كون القسم أخص من المقسم مطلقا، بل قسمة ما هو الموجد منهما فلا يكون العدم من الأمور العامة قطعا، إذ ليس يتحقق في موجود خارجي أصلا فضلا عن أن يوجد في أكثر من قسم منه وأما القدم فإن أريد به القدم الذاتي فظاهر أنه ليس من الأمور العامة وإن أريد عدم المسبوقية بالعدم فعدم عده منها مبني على أن الصفات ليست من الأعراض، إذ لو عدت منها وأريد بها هاهنا ما لا يقوم بنفسها كما ينبئ عنه حصرهم أقسام الموجود في الثلاثة سيما على القول ببقاء الأعراض كما يقول : بعض المتكلمين كان القدم المطلق من الأمور العامة بالتفسير المذكور. قوله : (ما يتناول المفهومات بأسرها وهذا هو المناسب بمعنى العموم وعموم الموضوع للموجود والمعدوم على ما اختاره المصنف
The Necessary, may He be exalted, meaning that His existence is from the cause. I say: after conceding that what is meant is not being effect for another, being effect is absolutely not conceivable in the Necessary, because the cause of need for a cause is either temporal origination or possibility, and the effect, on the assumption of the superaddition of His existence, is the qualification by existence which is possible in itself, not the self itself which is the Necessary. The commentator will explicitly state this in the third section of this position. So building the second answer upon it, but the discussion regarding this answer is a place for reflection, so reflect. That is prevented. Then his saying: (Based on this, nonexistence, impossibility, essential necessity, and eternity are not among the universal matters) – regarding nonexistence, it is claimed that it is among the common states between the two, namely substance and accident, because what is meant by substance is a quiddity which, if it existed in the external, would be not in a subject, and likewise what is meant by accident is a quiddity which, if it existed in the external, would be in a subject. That is supported by the fact that the author entitled the first chapter 'Existence and Nonexistence' together. And you are aware that if a thing is conceded to be qualified by substantiality or accidentalness in the state of nonexistence, then absolute substance and absolute accident would not be divisions of the external existent, based on the truth that the division must be more specific than the divided absolutely. Rather, the division is of what exists from them. Thus nonexistence is certainly not among the universal matters, since it is not realized in any external existent at all, let alone being present in more than one division of it. As for eternity, if what is meant by it is essential eternity, then it is obvious that it is not among the universal matters. If what is meant is lack of being preceded by nonexistence, then its not being counted among them is based on the fact that the attributes are not accidents; for if they were counted among them and what is meant here by them is what does not subsist by itself, as their limiting the divisions of the existent to three indicates, especially on the view of the persistence of accidents as some theologians hold, then absolute eternity would be among the universal matters according to the mentioned interpretation. His saying: (What encompasses the concepts entirely) and this is what is appropriate, meaning the universality and the universality of the subject for both the existent and the nonexistent, according to what the author chose.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of universal matters, beginning with a clarification about the Necessary Being and the concept of 'being an effect' (ma'luliyyah). The commentator notes that if by 'the Necessary' we mean that His existence is from a cause, then after conceding that it is not being an effect for another, being an effect is absolutely not conceivable in the Necessary because need for a cause is due to temporal origination or possibility; the effect in the case of superaddition of existence is the qualification by existence, not the essence itself. This point will be elaborated later. The commentator then returns to the claim that nonexistence, impossibility, essential necessity, and eternity are not universal matters. He addresses an objection that nonexistence might be a common state between substance and accident, based on the definitions of substance and accident as quiddities that would be not in a subject or in a subject if they existed. The objector points out that the author entitled the first chapter 'Existence and Nonexistence' together, implying nonexistence is a common consideration. The commentator refutes this: if a thing is described as substance or accident in the state of nonexistence, then absolute substance and accident would not be divisions of the external existent (since divisions must be more specific than the divided). Actually, the division is of what exists, so nonexistence is not realized in any external existent, let alone being found in more than one division. Then he discusses eternity: if essential eternity (qidam dhati) is meant, it is clearly not universal. If lack of being preceded by nonexistence is meant, its not being universal depends on whether attributes are considered accidents. If attributes are considered accidents and what is meant by 'accident' here is what does not subsist by itself (as indicated by limiting divisions of the existent to three), then on the view that accidents persist, absolute eternity (meaning lack of being preceded by nonexistence) could be universal. The passage ends with a definition: universal matters are what encompass all concepts (al-mafhumat), i.e., the Necessary, the possible, and the impossible. This is appropriate, meaning the subject is universal for both the existent and the nonexistent, as the author chose.

Key Terms
'adam (عدم)
imtina' (امتناع)
jawhar (جوهر)
'arad (عرض)
umur 'ammah (أمور عامة)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator argue that nonexistence cannot be a universal matter?
A) Because it is not a concept.
B) Because it is not realized in any external existent.
C) Because it only applies to the Necessary.
D) Because it is impossible to define.

Key Takeaway
Nonexistence is not a universal matter because it does not exist in any actual existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that nonexistence is not a real thing that exists outside your mind. How often do you let thoughts of what you lack (wealth, status, relationships) consume you, as if those nonexistent things were real? What would change if you truly accepted that only what Allah has brought into existence matters?
?
This passage emphasizes that universal matters must be found in actual existents. Consider your own life: Are you chasing illusions — 'what could have been' or 'what might be' — rather than engaging with the reality Allah has placed before you? What specific action can you take today to ground yourself in what actually exists?
?
The commentator argues that nonexistence cannot be a common state because it has no external reality. Think about the 'nonexistence' you fear most — a loss, a failure, a missed opportunity. In light of this lesson, is it wise to let a non-existent possibility dictate your present? How can you redirect your heart to the existent blessings of Allah?

The Status of Eternity and the Definition of Universal Matters
Commentator discusses eternity and defines universal matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of eternity and the final definition of universal matters as encompassing all concepts.

Core Idea
Eternity can be a universal matter only if interpreted as lack of precedence by nonexistence and if attributes are considered accidents.

The commentator continues examining the claim that eternity is not a universal matter, distinguishing two meanings. He then concludes with the definition of universal matters as what encompasses all concepts, including the Necessary, possible, and impossible. This final definition ties together the entire discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the shift from the discussion of eternity to the definition.
2
Distinguish the two meanings of eternity
Essential eternity (qidam dhati) is clearly not universal. Lack of being preceded by nonexistence might be universal under certain conditions.
3
Understand the condition for eternity to be universal
The condition is that attributes are considered accidents, and some theologians accept the persistence of accidents. If so, absolute eternity could be a universal attribute.
4
Absorb the final definition of universal matters
Universal matters are what encompass all concepts (mafhumat), i.e., the most general categories like existence, nonexistence, necessity, possibility, etc. This includes both existent and nonexistent.
This connects to the learning objective.

Four Possibilities on the Non-Existent and the State
Classifies the four possible positions on the non-existent and the state

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical possibilities regarding the non-existent and the intermediate state (al-ḥāl), and how they derive from two binary choices.

Core Idea
The four positions on the non-existent and the state are exhaustive logical combinations.

This passage lays out the foundational classification that structures the debate in kalam about the nature of non-existence and the intermediate state. By understanding these four possibilities, you will see how different theological schools divide on these fundamental questions. The text presents two binary choices that yield four exhaustive positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall meaning.
2
Identify the two binary choices
Find the two pairs of alternatives: (1) the non-existent is fixed or not, (2) the intermediate state is affirmed or not.
These are the key distinctions that generate the four positions.
3
Understand the logical combinations
Realize that the two choices produce four exhaustive combinations: (fixed, affirmed), (fixed, not affirmed), (not fixed, affirmed), (not fixed, not affirmed).
Each combination corresponds to a possible doctrine held by a group.
4
Reflect on the significance
Note that the author uses this classification to organize the discussion of schools. Each school's view is one of these four possibilities.
This shows how kalam thinkers systematically map out positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموقف الثاني : في الأمور العامة
الاحتمال أربع تقسيمات مبنية على مذاهبهم الأربعة وبيان ذلك أنه (إما أن يقال: بأن المعدوم ثابت أولا وعلى التقديرين إما أن تثبت الواسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم وهو الحال أو لا فهذه أربعة احتمالات) ذهب إلى كل واحد منها طائفة منهم .
The Second Position: On General Matters. The possibility: four divisions based on their four schools, and the explanation of that is that (either it is said that the non-existent is fixed or not, and in both cases either the intermediate between the existent and the non-existent, which is the state (al-ḥāl), is affirmed or not; so these are four possibilities). Each group among them adhered to one of them.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a classification. The author states that there are four possible positions (iḥtimālāt) on the nature of the non-existent (al-maʿdūm) and the existence of an intermediate state (al-ḥāl) between existence and non-existence. These four possibilities are derived from two binary choices: first, whether the non-existent has a fixed reality (thābit) or not; second, whether an intermediate state between the existent and the non-existent is affirmed or not. Each of the four schools holds one of these positions. This sets up the framework for the subsequent discussion.

Key Terms
المعدوم
الموجود
الحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the four possibilities are arbitrary or that there could be more than four.
Correction: The passage shows these are the only exhaustive possibilities derived from two logical binary choices.

Checkpoint
How many possibilities are there regarding the non-existent and the intermediate state?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The four positions on the non-existent and the state are derived from two binary choices, making them exhaustive and logically complete.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider matters of faith, do you approach them with the same comprehensive logical method as the scholars who enumerated all possibilities? What is one theological issue where you have only considered one side without exploring all logical options?
?
This lesson shows that our limited human minds can still systematically categorize possibilities about reality and God. How does this encourage you to trust your intellect as a gift from Allah in understanding your creed? What step will you take to use reason more consciously in your aqeedah?
?
The fact that the 'state' is an intermediate between existence and non-existence challenges our usual binary thinking. Can you think of a concept in your own life (like intention, doubt, or hope) that is not simply 'present' or 'absent'? How might recognizing an intermediate state deepen your relationship with Allah?

Ahl al-Ḥaqq's Division of the Known into Non-Existent and Existent
Presents the first possibility and divides the known into non-existent and existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first possibility regarding the non-existent and the binary division of the known into non-existent and existent according to Ahl al-Ḥaqq.

Core Idea
Ahl al-Ḥaqq divides the known into non-existent and existent, with no intermediate.

This lesson introduces the first of several possibilities concerning the status of the non-existent. According to Ahl al-Ḥaqq (the Ashʿarīs), the non-existent has no fixed reality, and there is no intermediate state between existence and non-existence. The category of the known is thus divided into two: that which has no actuality in the external world (the non-existent) and that which does (the existent). This binary division forms the foundation for further subdivisions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the key claim: 'the non-existent is not fixed.'
Pay attention to the use of parentheses in the Arabic.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage divides the known into non-existent and existent. Notice that the qualification 'in the external' is used because they do not affirm mental existence.
The division is based on actuality in the external world.
3
Understand the context
This is the position of Ahl al-Ḥaqq. Consider why they reject an intermediate state between existence and non-existence.
This is a key point of contention with other schools.
4
Reflect on the implications
This binary division leads to further ternary and quaternary divisions in the text. Try to anticipate what those might be.
The passage explicitly mentions that it is followed by other divisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الأول المعدوم ليس بثابت ولا واسطة أيضاً بينهما (وهو مذهب أهل الحق فالمعلوم ) أي ما من شأنه أن يعلم ) إما أن لا يكون له تحقق في الخارج إنما اعتبر قيد في الخارج لأنهم لا يقولون بالوجود الذهني ( أو يكون والأول ) هو (المعدوم) في الخارج ( والثاني ) هو ( الموجود ) فيه فهذه قسمة ثنائية يتبعها ثلاثيتان ورباعية .
(The first: The non-existent is not fixed, and there is no intermediate between them either, and it is the school of the people of truth (Ahl al-Ḥaqq). So the known, i.e. that which is apt to be known, is either that which has no actuality in the external (they only considered the qualification in the external because they do not affirm mental existence) or it does. The first is the non-existent in the external, and the second is the existent in it. So this is a binary division followed by two ternary and one quaternary divisions.)
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage defines the first possibility: the non-existent has no fixed reality and there is no intermediate state. This is the position of Ahl al-Ḥaqq (the Ashʿarīs). They then divide the known (al-maʿlūm) into two categories: that which has no actuality in the external world (al-maʿdūm) and that which does (al-mawjūd). The author notes that they only consider external actuality because they do not affirm mental existence. This binary division is then extended to ternary and quaternary divisions in subsequent analyses.

Correction: It does not mean restriction; rather, it is a necessary condition for the division because they do not affirm mental existence.

Definition of Hal and Its Explanation
Commentator defines hal and explains each part of its definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of 'hal' (state) as an intermediate ontological category and the function of each qualifier in the definition.

Core Idea
Hal is a dependent attribute, neither fully existent nor non-existent.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the definition of 'hal' (state) by explaining each part of the formal description: 'an attribute of an existent, not existent, not non-existent.' Understanding these qualifiers is essential to grasp how kalam distinguishes states from substances, accidents, and negations. Let's work through each element carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the dense technical style.
The passage contains a definition and then commentary on each part.
2
Identify the four qualifiers
The definition has four parts: (1) 'attribute of an existent', (2) 'not existent', (3) 'not non-existent'. The commentator also discusses the meaning of 'attribute' as subsisting in another. Identify each part and what it excludes.
3
Understand the exclusions
See what each qualifier excludes: 'attribute' excludes substances; 'of an existent' excludes non-existent referents; 'not existent' excludes accidents; 'not non-existent' excludes negations. Ask yourself why each exclusion is necessary.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
The common misunderstanding is that a 'hal' is a fully existent entity. The definition clarifies its dependent existence. Think about why this matters for theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وعرفوه) خرج من التقسيم تعريف الحال أنه معلوم يكون تحققه تبعاً لغيره، ولا خفاء في أن التعريفين متلازمان . قوله : (صفة لموجود) سواء كان موجوداً قبل قيام هذه الصفة أو معه، فيدخل الوجود عند القائل بأنه حال . قوله: (وهي الأمور القائمة بأنفسها فالمراد بالصفة ما يكون قائماً بغيره بمعنى الاختصاص الناعت، فتدخل الأجناس والفصول في الأحوال والأحوال القائمة بذاته تعالى كالعالمية والقادرية عند من يثبتها . قولنا : (لا موجودة لتخرج الأعراض) فإنها متحققة باعتبار ذواتها فهي من قبيل الموجود دون الحال وقولنا : ( ولا معدومة لتخرج السلوب ) التي يتصف بها الموجود فإنها معدومة لا أحوال.
His statement: (And they defined it) came from the division: the definition of al-hal (state) is that it is something known whose realization is dependent upon something else, and there is no hiddenness in that the two definitions are correlative. His statement: (an attribute of an existent) whether the existent existed before the subsistence of this attribute or with it, so existence enters according to the one who says it is a hal (state). His statement: (And they are the self-subsistent things) so what is meant by attribute is that which subsists in something else in the sense of specifying characterization, so genera and differentiae enter into the states and the states subsisting in His essence, exalted, such as being Knowing and being Powerful according to those who affirm them. Our statement: (not existent) to exclude accidents, for they are realized with respect to their own essences, so they belong to the category of the existent, not the hal (state). And our statement: (and not non-existent) to exclude negations which the existent is described by, for they are non-existent, not states.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage provides and explains the definition of 'hal' (state) as an intermediate ontological category between existence and non-existence. The commentator begins by noting that the definition of hal emerges from a prior division: it is something known whose realization is dependent on something else (i.e., it exists only as an attribute of another entity). He then explains each part of the formal definition: 'attribute of an existent, not existent, not non-existent.' First, 'attribute' (صفة) means what subsists in something else, thereby excluding self-subsistent substances (ذوات). Second, 'of an existent' (لموجود) indicates that the attribute belongs to something that exists, even if the attribute itself is not considered existent in its own right; this excludes attributes of non-existent things. Third, 'not existent' (لا موجودة) excludes accidents, which are considered existent in themselves even though they inhere. Fourth, 'not non-existent' (ولا معدومة) excludes negations (سلوب), which are non-existent. The commentator also clarifies that the attribute can subsist in an existent that existed before or simultaneously with the attribute, and that psychological attributes like being Knowing and being Powerful (for God) are included as states.

Key Terms
الحال
صفة
الذوات
الأعراض
السلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A 'hal' is a fully existent, independent entity.
Correction: A hal is an attribute whose existence is dependent on another; it is neither fully existent like a substance nor non-existent like a negation.

Checkpoint
According to the definition, what does the qualifier 'not existent' (لا موجودة) exclude?
A) Substances (ذوات)
B) Accidents (أعراض)
C) Negations (سلوب)
D) Attributes of non-existent things

Key Takeaway
The definition of hal uses four qualifiers to clearly distinguish it from substances, accidents, and negations.
