Objection on Parts of Existence: Simultaneity and Posteriority
Objection that parts are simultaneous or posterior to the whole, with a problem of self-difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an objection against the priority of parts in existence and the logical problem of self-difference it raises.

Core Idea
Even if parts exist, they cannot be prior to the whole without leading to paradox.

This passage presents a critical objection to the idea that existence has parts. The objector argues that even if parts existed, their relation to the whole would be simultaneous or posterior, not prior. This raises a deeper problem: in both cases, the part would be different from itself or involve self-precedence. Let's examine the objection carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure of the objection and the commentator's interjection.
The passage is cut off at the end; we only have the beginning of the response.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The objector presents two possibilities for the part's existence relative to the whole: simultaneous (مع) or posterior (بعد). Understand that in both, the part is not prior.
The objector uses these to argue that the part cannot be prior to the whole.
3
Understand the commentator's remark
The commentator points out an additional corruption: in simultaneity, the thing would differ from itself; in posteriority, there is also priority involved. Reflect on why this is a problem.
The full explanation of this corruption is cut off; consider it as an open question.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال : لو كان للوجود أجزاء فتلك الأجزاء إما أن تتصف بوجود مع أو بعد أي مع الوجود الذي هو المركب أو بعده فليس الجزء بحسب وجوده متقدماً على كله بل هو إما معه أو متأخر عنه. قوله : (فليس الجزء متقدماً) فإن قلت : فيهما فساد آخر غير ما ذكر بناء على أن في المعية الذاتية مغايرة الشيء لنفسه وفي البعدية تلك المغايرة مع التقدم كما في القبلية، فلم لم يتعرض له قلت
And it may be said: if existence had parts, then those parts are either characterized by existence together with [the whole] or after it – i.e., together with the existence that is the composite, or after it – so the part, in respect of its existence, is not prior to its whole; rather it is either with it or posterior to it. His statement: (so the part is not prior). If you say: in both there is another corruption besides what was mentioned, based on the fact that in essential simultaneity there is a difference of the thing from itself, and in posteriority there is that difference along with priority, as in priority – so why did he not address it? I say:
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents another objection to the claim that existence has parts. The objector says: even if existence had parts, those parts could be characterized by existence either simultaneously with the whole existence or after it, but not necessarily before it. Thus, the part is not prior to the whole in existence; it is either simultaneous or posterior. The commentator then notes a point: there is another corruption in both scenarios beyond what was mentioned. In essential simultaneity, the thing would be different from itself (since the part's existence coincides with the whole's existence, but the whole is composed of the part, so they are distinct yet simultaneous). In posteriority, there is also a kind of priority involved. The commentator asks why the author did not address this, and then begins to respond (but the text cuts off).

الوجود
الأجزاء
المعية
البعدية
المغايرة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that temporal simultaneity or posteriority avoids the problem of part priority.
Correction: The passage shows that essential priority leads to self-precedence even in those cases, as the part would differ from itself or involve priority alongside posteriority.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Second Argument for Negating Composition of Existence: Analysis and Answer
Commentary on second argument against composition of existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against the composition of existence, the illustrative example of the house, and the philosophers' answer that existence and non-existence are secondary intelligibles.

Core Idea
Composition arguments collapse when existence is understood as a secondary intelligible.

This passage tackles a key objection to the unity of existence. The second argument attempts to show that composition leads to contradiction, but the philosophers respond by clarifying the status of existence and non-existence as mental constructs. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. The opening vinegar example is a base text being commented on. Then read the translation to get the flow.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the core argument
The argument states that parts of any composite must be described either by existence or non-existence. Both options lead to problems: either the whole becomes an attribute of the part, or a conjunction of opposites follows.
This is a classic dilemma.
3
Consider the house analogy
The commentator uses the example of a house: its parts are either a house or not a house. This clarifies how the dilemma works in a tangible case.
The example makes the abstract reasoning concrete.
4
Understand the philosophers' answer
The truth is that both existence and non-existence are themselves described by non-existence and are secondary intelligibles—mental concepts without external reality. This dissolves the apparent contradiction.
The answer shifts the framework from realism to conceptualism about these predicates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان السكنجبين محض ما ليس بسكنجبين (قوله : ( في الاستدلال ثانياً على نفي تركيب الوجود ( الأجزاء تتصف بالوجود أو العدم قلنا كسائر المركبات ( المعلومة التركيب ( إذ أجزاؤها لا تخلو عنها أو عن نقيضها)، فيكون الدليل منقوضاً بها إذ نقول : مثلا أجزاء الدار إما دار أو ليست بدار، فعلى الأول يكون الكل صفة للجزء، وعلى الثاني يلزم اجتماع النقيضين ) والحق عند الحكماء اتصاف الوجود ونقيضه) أي العدم ( بالعدم وإنه ) أي الوجود بل العدم أيضاً ( من المعقولات الثانية التي لا وجود لها
قوله: (لا يخلو عنها وعن نقيضها أي عن الاتصاف بها أو عن الاتصاف بنقيضها في الوجود ، ولا يلزم جريان جميع الوجوه المذكورة.
قوله : إما) (دار) أي تتصف بدار أو تتصف بلیست بدار. قوله : (يلزم اجتماع النقيضين) بالوجه الأول من الوجوه المذكورة سابقاً في كونه نقيضاً. قوله : ( والحق إلخ ) جواب عن الاستدلال الثاني بطريق الحل . قوله : (بل العدم إلخ ) أشار بالإضراب إلى أن تخصيص الوجود بالحكم لكون الكلام فيه لا لنفيه عن العدم.
There was the pure vinegar, nothing but [it is] not vinegar. (His saying: (In the second argument for negating the composition of existence (The parts are described by existence or non-existence. We say: As with other composite things (known composition), since their parts are not free from it or from its opposite, so the proof is refuted by them; for we say: For example, parts of a house are either a house or not a house. On the first assumption, the whole becomes an attribute of the part; on the second, the conjunction of two opposites follows.) And the truth according to the philosophers is that existence and its opposite – meaning non-existence – are described by non-existence, and that it – meaning existence, indeed also non-existence – is from the secondary intelligibles which have no existence. His saying: ('not free from it and from its opposite'), meaning from being described by it or from being described by its opposite in existence, and it does not necessitate the running of all the aforementioned aspects. His saying: ('either') ('a house'), meaning it is described as a house or it is described as not a house. His saying: ('the conjunction of two opposites follows') according to the first of the aforementioned aspects concerning its being an opposite. His saying: ('And the truth etc.') is an answer to the second argument by way of resolution. His saying: ('indeed non-existence etc.') indicated by the shift that the specification of existence with the ruling is because the discussion is about it, not because of negating it from non-existence.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the second argument for negating the composition of existence. It begins with an illustrative example: 'pure vinegar is nothing but not vinegar,' which is a base text being commented on. The commentator then presents the argument: parts of existence must be described either by existence or non-existence. If they are described by existence, then the whole (existence) becomes an attribute of the part (problematic). If they are described by non-existence, then the whole (existence) would have the attribute of non-existence, leading to a contradiction (conjunction of opposites). This is compared to the example of a house: parts of a house are either a house or not a house; if they are a house, the whole is an attribute of the part; if not, contradiction follows. The commentator then states that the truth according to the philosophers is that both existence and non-existence are themselves described by non-existence, and both are secondary intelligibles that have no external existence. The remaining lines are explanations of the wording: 'not free from it and its opposite' means not free from being described by it or its opposite; the example of the house is clarified; the conjunction of opposites is based on one aspect; and the phrase 'and the truth etc.' is an answer via resolution. The shift to 'indeed non-existence' indicates that specifying existence with the ruling is due to the focus of discussion, not to negate it from non-existence.

Key Terms

عدم
تركيب
معقولات ثانية
نقيض
حكماء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The composition argument proves an actual contradiction in existence.
Correction: The answer shows that the contradiction arises only if we treat existence and non-existence as real external attributes; as secondary intelligibles, no real contradiction exists.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is the status of existence and non-existence?
A) They are both real external attributes of things.
B) They are both secondary intelligibles with no external existence.
C) Existence is real but non-existence is not.
D) Non-existence is real but existence is not.

Key Takeaway
The second argument against composition of existence is resolved by recognizing that existence and non-existence are not real external attributes but secondary intelligibles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own existence is just a mental concept (a secondary intelligible), then what is truly real about you? How does this realization push you to seek a reality that is not merely conceptual but comes directly from Allah?
?
You have seen how a logical argument can be resolved by redefining terms. Are there areas in your faith where you rely on human reasoning rather than divine revelation? What would it take for you to surrender your intellect to the truth of the Quran and Sunnah even when it seems paradoxical?
?
The philosophers claim that existence is not a real attribute. Yet you experience your existence as the most immediate fact. Do you trust your experience more than this proof? Or do you accept that your perception may be deceptive? How does this lesson change the way you think about the ultimate reality of your actions and their weight on the Day of Judgment?

Objection and Rebuttal of the Part-Whole Example
Commentary on the objection and its rebuttal via whole-part conjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that describing a part by non-existence does not entail the whole being non-existent, and how the commentator rebuts it by invoking the conjunction of a whole with its part.

Core Idea
The part-whole objection is refuted by the logical necessity of whole-part conjunction.

In this passage, the commentator addresses an objection to the second argument for composition. The opponent claims that the house example does not require the conjunction of opposites because describing a part of the house as non-house does not make the whole non-house. The commentator shows that this objection fails because the whole necessarily conjoins with its parts, so if a part has a property, the whole is affected.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic text and translation from the beginning up to 'فلا نقض' to see the objection that describing a part by negation does not entail the whole being negated.
2
Study the rebuttal
Read the following section starting from 'ويمكن أن يقال' to see how the commentator argues that the conjunction of opposites still follows because the whole conjoins with the part.
3
Analyze the logical chain
Notice that the rebuttal works by showing that if a part is described by a property, then the whole, which includes that part, must also be described by that property, leading to the original absurdity.
This is a key move in kalam argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وعلى الثاني يلزم اجتماع النقيضين فيه بحث لأن لزوم اجتماع النقيضين على تقدير أن يتصف أجزاء الوجود بالعدم، كان باعتبار أن اتصاف الجزء بالعدم يستلزم اتصاف الكل الذي هو الوجود به وهذا غير متأت في صورة الدار لأن اتصاف جزء من الدار بسلبها لا يقتضي اتصاف كلها به فلا نقض ويمكن أن يقال: إذا كان جزء الدار متصفا بسلب الدار ولا شك أن الكل يجتمع مع الجزء وأن اجتماع الموصوف بشيء يستلزم اجتماع صفته به يلزم اجتماع النقيضين وهذا الوجه يجري في صورة الوجود أيضاً، فإن بني المستدل لزوم اجتماع النقيضين على تقدير اتصاف أجزاء الوجود بالعدم على هذا، فالأمر ظاهر وإن بناه على ما ذكرته من أن يستلزم عدم الكل لم يقدح في ورود النقض أيضاً، لأن مقدمات الدليل جارية في صورة النقض، والمخالفة في تعليل إحدى المقدمات لا في نفسها، وهذا القدر لا يضر في النقض، وبهذا ظهر ضعف ما اختاره الشارح في كتبه المنطقية دفعاً لاعتراض لزوم اشتراط الشيء بنقيضه أو تقومه بالنقيضين على تقدير اعتبار التصور الساذج في التصديق من أن المعتبر في التصديق شرطاً أو شطراً هو ذات التصور الساذج لا مفهوم العارض لتلك الذات، فلا يرد شيء من المحذورين وذلك لأن المحذور في اشتراط الشيء بنقيضه وتقومه بالنقيضين ليس إلا اجتماع النقيضين، وعدم الحكم إذا كان عارضاً لازماً لما صدق عليه التصور، فإذا اجتمع هذا التصور المفروض مع الحكم يلزم اجتماع عارضه اللازم معه فيعود أصل الفساد سواء كان العارض جزءاً أو شرطاً أم لا، فالجواب الحق عن ذلك الاعتراض هو الذي لم يلتفت إليه القوم على ما نقله في حاشية المطالع فتأمل . قوله : ( وإنه أي الوجود بل العدم أيضا من المعقولات الثانية إلخ) أشار بقوله بل العدم إلى وجه تأويل إفراد الضمير مع أن الظاهر فإنهما لاقتضاء السياق رجوعه إليهما، وهاهنا بحث وهو أن
His saying: (And on the second [assumption], the conjunction of two opposites follows.) There is a discussion regarding it: because the necessity of the conjunction of two opposites on the assumption that parts of existence are described by non-existence was considered from the angle that the description of the part by non-existence necessitates the description of the whole, which is existence, by it; and this is not attainable in the case of the house, because the description of a part of the house by its negation does not necessitate the description of all of it by it, so there is no refutation. And it could be said: If a part of the house is described by the negation of the house, and there is no doubt that the whole conjoins with the part, and that the conjunction of what is described by something necessitates the conjunction of its attribute with it, then the conjunction of two opposites follows; and this aspect applies also in the case of existence. So if the arguer built the necessity of the conjunction of two opposites on the assumption of the description of parts of existence by non-existence on this [basis], then the matter is clear; and if he built it on what I mentioned, that it necessitates the non-existence of the whole, it does not harm the occurrence of the refutation either, because the premises of the proof apply in the case of the refutation, and the disagreement is in the explanation of one of the premises, not in the premise itself. This much does not harm the refutation. And by this is shown the weakness of what the commentator chose in his logical books to repel the objection of the necessity of conditioning a thing by its opposite or its constitution by both opposites, on the assumption of considering the simple concept in assent: that what is considered in assent as condition or part is the essence of the simple concept, not the notion of the accident of that essence. So neither of the two harms occurs. That is because the harm in conditioning a thing by its opposite and its constitution by both opposites is nothing but the conjunction of two opposites; and the absence of judgment, if it is an inseparable accident of what the concept applies to, then when this supposed concept comes together with the judgment, the conjunction of its necessary accident with it follows, so the original corruption returns, whether the accident is a part or a condition or not. So the true answer to that objection is that which the people did not pay attention to, as transmitted in the margin of al-Mutala' (so reflect). His saying: (And that it, i.e., existence, indeed non-existence also, from the secondary intelligibles, etc.) he indicated by his saying 'indeed non-existence' the aspect of interpreting the singular pronoun, even though the apparent [wording] is that they [both] are [the referent] due to the context requiring returning to both. And here there is a discussion: that
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that first notes a discussion about the preceding objection to the second argument. The objection claims that the conjunction of two opposites does not necessarily follow in the case of the house example, because describing a part of the house by non-existence does not entail the whole (the house) being described by non-existence; hence the refutation does not hold. The commentator then offers a possible reply: if a part of the house is described by non-house, and the whole conjoins with the part, then the conjunction of opposites follows. This same logic applies to existence. So if the arguer bases his necessity on this reasoning, the objection is resolved; if he bases it on the entailment of non-existence of the whole, it also does not refute the argument because the premises of the original proof still apply. The commentator then critiques a position taken by 'the commentator' (probably referring to the author of the commentary himself or another) in his logical works, which attempted to avoid an objection about conditioning by opposites by considering the simple concept in assent. The commentator argues that this approach fails because the core issue is the conjunction of opposites, which recurs. The true answer, he says, is the one he alludes to, as found in the margin of al-Mutala'. He then comments on a phrase about existence and non-existence being secondary intelligibles, explaining the use of the singular pronoun and indicating there is further discussion.

اجتماع النقيضين
نقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the house example successfully refutes the composition argument because a part can be non-house without the whole being non-house.
Correction: The commentator shows that the whole necessarily conjoins with the part, so if a part is non-house, the whole must also be non-house, thus preserving the absurdity.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the commentator gives for rejecting the objection based on the house example?
A) The house is not like existence because it is composite.
B) The whole conjoins with the part, so the attribute of the part transfers to the whole.
C) The objection confuses parts and wholes in a logical fallacy.
D) The commentator accepts the objection but modifies the argument.

Key Takeaway
The part-whole objection fails because the whole necessarily shares the attribute of any part, leading to the same absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a small fault in your character that you consider isolated and insignificant. According to the logic of this passage, does your overall integrity (the 'whole') remain unaffected? What does this demand of you today?
?
If you claim that one area of your life (e.g., your worship) is sound while another (e.g., your dealings with people) is flawed, how does the principle of attribute transfer challenge your compartmentalization? Will you allow this lesson to break down that division?
?
The passage shows that describing a part with a quality forces the whole to share it. What 'part' of your daily routine is currently described by negligence or heedlessness? Are you ready to admit that this affects your entire spiritual state, and what will you change right now?

Critique of the Simple Concept Solution and True Answer
Critique of an alternative solution and mention of the correct answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the solution based on the simple concept in assent is flawed and what the true answer to the objection is.

Core Idea
The simple concept solution fails because the inseparable accident still causes the conjunction of opposites.

After addressing the immediate objection, the commentator critiques a position found in logical books that attempts to avoid the problem by considering only the simple concept itself, not its accidents. He argues that this does not work because the core issue is the conjunction of opposites, which recurs through the inseparable accident of non-judgment. He then hints at the true answer as transmitted in the margin of al-Mutala'.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique of the simple concept solution
Read from 'و بهذا ظهر ضعف ما اختاره الشارح' to 'فهذا القدر لا يضر في النقض' to understand the commentator's rejection of this solution.
2
Understand the reason for failure
The commentator explains that the harm (conjunction of opposites) arises from the inseparable accident of non-judgment, which remains even if we consider only the simple concept.
3
Learn the true answer
The commentator states that the true answer is the one he alludes to, as found in the margin of al-Mutala'. Reflect on what this might be.
The text does not give the true answer explicitly here.
4
Note the secondary intelligibles remark
Read the final part about existence and non-existence being secondary intelligibles, and note the discussion about the pronoun.
This opens another line of inquiry.

The Third Argument Against Composition and Its Presuppositions
Discusses the third argument against composition and its reliance on genus-species distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two metaphysical assumptions required for the third argument against composition of existence—namely, the real distinctness and priority of genus and species—and why these assumptions are denied.

Core Idea
The argument against composition depends on contested assumptions about genus and species.

This passage continues the critique of arguments for divine simplicity. The third argument claims that if existence were composed, its parts would have to relate to the whole in a contradictory time-sequence. But the commentator shows this reasoning is not self-standing: it relies on two unproven claims about genus and species. Understanding these assumptions reveals why the argument fails, and leads to deeper discussions about the nature of states (aḥwāl) and the intermediary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the full translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the structure: the argument's foundation, the intermediary, and the objection.
2
Identify the two assumptions
The passage explicitly states the argument is built on two things: the distinctness (tamayyuz) of genus and species, and their priority over the species (naw‘). Find these two and note how they are defined.
If genus and species are not distinct in external reality, the argument's disjunction fails.
3
Understand the denial of both assumptions
The commentary states both matters are denied (mamnū‘ān). Consider why Muslim theologians reject these assumptions—they do not accept that jins and fasl are real, external universals with priority.
4
Examine the discussion on the intermediary and states
The passage then addresses an explicit affirmation of an intermediary (wāsita) and an objection from those who affirm states (aḥwāl). Read how the objection is repelled by al-Katibi's broader definition of 'in an existent'.
This shows the interconnectedness of arguments in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (هذا مبني إلخ) أي هذا القول إلى آخره أعني المنفصلة مع دليل إبطالها مبني على أمرين أحدهما تمايز الجنس والفصل، إذ على تقدير عدم التمايز نختار أن الأجزاء تتصف بالوجود الذي هو نفس وجود الكل والترديد المذكور إنما يتجه إذا كان وجودها مغايراً لوجوده، مقدماً والثاني تقدمهما على النوع فإن إبطال المعية والتأخر بقوله : فليس الجزء بحسب الوجود على كله مبني على ذلك وكلا الأمرين ممنوعان .
قوله : ( وهو تصريح بإثبات الواسطة المقدمة القائلة بأن الوجود لا يرد عليه القسمة قد صححها الشارح في حاشية شرح التجريد وأبطل توهم لزوم القول بالواسطة من هذا الكلام، فليطالع ثمة وقد أشرنا الآن إلى توجيه آخر لئلا يلزم الواسطة فلا تغفل .
قوله : ( فلا يصح إلا على رأي مثبتي الأحوال ) قال بعض الأفاضل: لكن ينافي تفسيرهم الحال بأنه صفة قائمة بموجود لأن الأجزاء حينئذ قائمة بما قام به الوجود الذي هو الكل، ولا شيء منها بقائم بموجود اللهم إلا أن يجاب بما أجاب به الكاتبي وأنت خبير باندفاع هذا السؤال بما حققناه في تعريف الحال من أن المراد بالموجود فيه أعم من الموجود، قبل قيام هذه الصفة أو معه وليس المراد الأول فقط حتى يرد ما ذكره
His saying: (This is built etc.) i.e., this statement to its end—meaning the disjunction with its refutation—is built on two things: one is the distinctness of genus and species, because on the assumption of non-distinctness we choose that the parts are characterized by the existence which is the very existence of the whole, and the mentioned disjunction is only directed if their existence is different from its existence, prior to it. The second is the priority of both over the species, because the refutation of simultaneity and posteriority by his saying: 'So the part is not with respect to existence over the whole' is built on that, and both matters are denied. His saying: (And it is an explicit affirmation of the intermediary) The premise that existence is not subject to division has been corrected by the commentator in his gloss on Sharh al-Tajrid, and he refuted the illusion of necessitating the affirmation of an intermediary from this statement. So let it be consulted there, and we have now indicated another direction so that the intermediary is not necessitated, so do not be heedless. His saying: (So it is only valid on the view of those who affirm states) Some virtuous said: But it contradicts their interpretation of the state (hal) as an attribute subsisting in an existent, because the parts then subsist in that to which existence—which is the whole—subsists, and none of them subsists in an existent. Unless it is answered with what al-Katibi answered, and you are aware that this objection is repelled by what we have verified in the definition of the state: that what is meant by 'in an existent' is more general than the existing [thing], either before the subsistence of this attribute or with it, and what is meant is not only the first so that what he mentioned would apply.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage examines the third argument against composition of existence. The argument claims that parts of existence must be either simultaneous with, after, or before the whole; each alternative leads to absurdity, thus composition is impossible. The commentary explains that this argument depends on two presuppositions: (1) the real distinctness of genus (jins) and species (fasl) in external reality—if they are not distinct, one could simply say that parts are characterized by the very existence of the whole without temporal ordering; (2) the priority of genus and species over the specific individual (naw‘). Both are denied. The passage then turns to the explicit affirmation of an intermediary (wāsita) from the proponents of states. The commentator corrects a misinterpretation of an earlier statement, noting that the premise 'existence is not subject to division' has been defended elsewhere. Finally, an objection against the state theory is raised: if a state is an attribute subsisting in an existent, then parts of existence (as states) would need to subsist in something existent, but they are not themselves existent; the response (from al-Katibi) is that the term 'in an existent' is broader—it can mean before the attribute subsists or with it, thus the parts can be states without an existent subject before subsistence.

Key Terms
جنس (jins)
فصل (fasl)
نوع (naw')
تمايز (tamayyuz)
منفصلة (munfasila)
موجود (mawjud)
حال (hal)
كاتبي (al-Katibi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third argument against composition is a self-sufficient, decisive proof that existence cannot be composed.
Correction: The argument depends on two unproven assumptions about the real distinctness and priority of genus and species, which are denied by many theologians.

Checkpoint
What two assumptions does the commentary identify as necessary for the third argument against composition of existence?
A) The distinctness of genus and species, and the priority of both over the individual.
B) The simplicity of existence and the reality of non-existence.
C) The existence of states and the denial of absolute existence.
D) The priority of the whole over parts and the simultaneity of all parts.

Key Takeaway
The third argument against composition of existence is only valid if one accepts the real external distinctness and priority of genus and species—assumptions that are rejected in the Islamic theological tradition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen that even a powerful argument against composition rests on assumptions that may not be granted. Examine your own core beliefs about Allah's oneness: are they built on premises you have critically examined, or have you simply accepted them without verification? Will you commit to studying the foundations of tawhid with the same rigor shown here?
?
The scholars in this passage debated nuances like genus, species, and states. Do you treat the finer points of 'aqidah as unimportant technicalities, or do you realize that your understanding of Allah may depend on them? Are you willing to learn even the difficult parts of theology to know your Lord better?
?
When someone raises an objection against Islamic teachings, do you quickly dismiss it by assuming the argument is invalid, or do you take the time to examine its premises? This lesson shows that arguments must be evaluated on their assumptions, not just their conclusions. How can you develop the habit of fair-minded critical thinking in matters of faith starting today?

The Middle State Between Existence and Non-Existence
Sanusi discusses the objection and refutation regarding the middle state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that parts of existence are neither existent nor non-existent, how it implies a middle state (wāsita), and why this is only valid according to the school of those who affirm states (ahwāl).

Core Idea
The middle state is not mental; it is a real attribute on the school of states.

Theologians debated whether every entity must be either existent or non-existent. Some raised an objection: parts of existence themselves seem to be described by neither. This leads to the idea of a middle state (wāsita). In this lesson, we explore Sanusi's analysis of this objection and why it only holds for those who affirm states (ahwāl). The passage clarifies the difference between predication (ḥaml) and attribution (ʿurūḍ) and defends the notion of derivative realization.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the claim that parts of existence are neither existent nor non-existent. This is the doubt.
2
Understand the implication
The commentator says this implies a middle state (wāsita) between existent and non-existent. Ask yourself: why is that a problem? Only those who accept states (ahwāl) can accept this.
The school of states is associated with certain Ashʿarites.
3
Distinguish ḥaml and ʿurūḍ
The passage then refines the objection: if 'description' means predication (ḥaml), the claim holds; if it means attribution (ʿurūḍ), it does not. Try to grasp this distinction.
This is a technical point about how properties are ascribed.
4
Follow the refutation
Finally, the commentator refutes a counter-objection: the state must be a property of an existent. He argues that if something is not non-existent, it has derivative realization (taḥaqquq tabaʿī), making it a state.
5
Take the checkpoint
Test your understanding with the multiple-choice question. It checks the main condition for the validity of the middle state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( وقد يقال ) في حل الشبهة ( لا تتصف أجزاء الوجود ( لا) بهذا ولا بذاك ) أي لا بالوجود ولا بالعدم (وهو تصريح بإثبات الواسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم فلا يصح إلا على مذهب مثبتي الأحوال، فتكون أجزاء الوجود عندهم من قبيل الأحوال كما أن الوجود عندهم كذلك ( قوله: ( وهو تصريح إلخ ) إذا حمل الاتصاف على الحمل وأما إذا أريد به العروض فلا كما مر وأما ما قيل من أنه لا بد في الحال من كونها صفة لموجود وهو غير لازم مما ذكر، فليس بشيء لأنه إذا قيل: إنها ليست بمعدومة لا بد من القول بالتحقق التبعي، ولأنه قول بالواسطة بينهما ولا واسطة سوى الحال أصلاً فيكون حالاً.
His saying (and it may be said) in solving the doubt: (The parts of existence are not described (not) by this nor by that) meaning neither by existence nor by non-existence. (And it is an explicit affirmation of a middle state (wāsita) between the existent and the non-existent, so it is only valid according to the school of those who affirm states (ahwāl). Thus the parts of existence according to them are of the category of states, just as existence according to them is likewise.) His saying (and it is an explicit affirmation etc.): if the description is interpreted as predication (ḥaml), but if what is meant is attribution (ʿurūḍ), then no, as has passed. And as for what was said, that the state (ḥāl) must be a property of an existent, and that is not necessary from what was mentioned, it is nothing, because if it is said that they are not non-existent, one must affirm derivative realization (taḥaqquq tabaʿī), and because it is a statement of a middle state between them, and there is no intermediary except the state originally, so it becomes a state.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the parts of existence. The commentator addresses the doubt that parts of existence are described neither by existence nor by non-existence. This is interpreted as an explicit affirmation of a middle state (wāsita) between the existent and the non-existent, which is only valid on the school of those who affirm states (ahwāl), such that parts of existence become like states. The commentator then explains that if description is taken as predication (ḥaml) then it holds, but if it is taken as attribution (ʿurūḍ) it fails. He refutes the objection that a state must be a property of an existent by arguing that if something is not non-existent, it necessitates a derivative realization, and since there is no intermediary except the state, it becomes a state.

Key Terms

حال
مثبتو الأحوال
اتصاف
حمل
عروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The middle state (wāsita) is a mental concept or a mere logical possibility.
Correction: In the context, the middle state is a real attribute (ḥāl) on the school of those who affirm states. It is not merely mental but has a derivative realization (taḥaqquq tabaʿī).

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, the claim that parts of existence are neither existent nor non-existent implies a middle state (wāsita). On which theological school is this valid?
A) The Muʿtazila
B) The Ashʿarites who affirm states (ahwāl)
C) The philosophers
D) The Māturīdites

Key Takeaway
The claim of a middle state between existence and non-existence is only coherent within the framework of the school that affirms states (ahwāl).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own existence, do you find yourself tempted to reduce everything to either 'real' or 'unreal'? What would it mean for you to accept that some things—like your relationship with God or your intentions—are 'states' that are neither fully existent nor non-existent, yet still real and consequential?
?
The precision of terms like 'predication' and 'attribution' determines whether an entire theological position holds. In your own life, are there beliefs you hold that collapse or transform when you examine the precise meaning of the words you use?
?
If the middle state is not an independent thing but a derivative realization, then much of what you rely on for your faith—like God's attributes or your own actions—may not be 'things' in the usual sense. How does that unsettle or deepen your trust in God, who alone is truly existent?

Attribute of a Part is Not Part of the Composite
Commentator explains a logical principle with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of a part is not considered a part of the composite, and how this resolves a potential objection.

Core Idea
The attribute of a part does not become a component of the whole.

This brief passage addresses a subtle but important distinction in understanding composites and their attributes. The commentator clarifies that even though an attribute may subsist in a part, it does not follow that the attribute itself is a part of the composite. This distinction helps avoid a misunderstanding regarding examples like the form of bedness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the compact style of the commentary.
The passage is a commentary on a base text phrase.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the three key terms: attribute of the part (صفة الجزء), composite (المركب), and the example of bedness (الهيئة السريرية). Understand each term in context.
3
Understand the logical point
The commentator argues that the attribute of a part is not a part of the composite. He then refutes an objection using the bedness example. Reflect on why the objection fails.
The attribute as such is not a constituent; only the part itself is.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فإن صفة الجزء ليست إلخ) أي لا يلزم أن يكون جزءاً للمركب أي من حيث إنها صفة له وقائمة به، ليست جزءاً للمركب، فلا يرد أن الهيئة السريرية صفة للخشب مع أنها جزء للسرير.
His statement: (For the attribute of the part is not, etc.) i.e., it does not necessarily follow that it is a part of the composite, that is, insofar as it is an attribute of it and subsisting in it, it is not a part of the composite. So it is not objected that the bedness form is an attribute of the wood while it is a part of the bed.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a phrase in the base text. The commentator clarifies that the attribute of a part is not a part of the composite. Even though the attribute subsists in the part, it does not become a component of the whole. The objection that the form of bedness is an attribute of the wood and yet is a part of the bed is invalid because the form is not a part in the same sense; the attribute as such is not a constituent of the composite.

Key Terms
صفة الجزء
المركب
الهيئة السريرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that since an attribute resides in a part, the attribute itself becomes a part of the whole composite.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the attribute of a part is not a part of the composite; it is only a quality of the part, not a constituent of the whole.

Checkpoint

A) It is a part of the composite
B) It is not a part of the composite
C) It is identical to the part
D) It is the whole composite

Key Takeaway
The attribute of a part, in itself, is not a component of the composite.

Refutation of Attribute-Based Definitions of the Existent
Refutes attribute definitions of existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why specific attributes like 'passive', 'eternal', and 'originated' cannot be used to define the term 'existent'.

Core Idea
A definition of 'existent' must apply to everything that exists.

This lesson examines the first refutation in the passage: the attempt to define 'the existent' using attributes that are not universal. The author points out that 'passive' requires an external agent, 'eternal' implies no beginning, and 'originated' implies a beginning—these do not cover all existents. Therefore, they cannot be part of a valid definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening refutation
Read the passage where the author lists the three attributes: passive, eternal, and originated. Note that each is defined in a way that restricts its applicability.
The author uses a concise style. Each attribute is given a brief definition before being rejected.
2
Identify the universality requirement
Understand that a definition of 'existent' must be true of every existing thing. The three attributes fail because they introduce restrictions that are not universal.
For example, 'passive' only applies to things that receive an effect from another—not to the First Cause.
3
Apply the lesson to other potential definitions
Reflect on why other specific attributes would also fail. This teaches a key principle: definitions of fundamental terms must be free from non-universal features.
This principle is crucial in kalam and philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثاني : في أنه مشترك
والمنفعل موجود فيه أثر من الغير والقديم موجود لا أول له والحادث هاهنا موجود له أول، فلا يصح أخذ شيء منها في تعريف الموجود، وصحة العلم والإخبار إمكان وجودهما فالتعريف بها أيضا دوري.

قوله : ( موجود لا أول له ) فإن المعدوم الذي لا أول له يقال له أزلي . قوله : (هاهنا ) إنما قال هاهنا لأنه قد يطلق الحادث بمعنى المتجدد فيشمل المعدوم الذي له أول .
قوله : ( وصحة العلم والإخبار (الخ ) فإن معناها إمكان العلم والإخبار والإمكان لا يتعلق بشيء لا باعتبار وجوده في نفسه، أو وجوده لغيره فيكون معناه إمكان وجودهما .
قوله : ( وصحة العلم والإخبار إمكان وجودهما فيه بحث لأن الإمكان في قولك يمكن أن يعلم ويخبر عنه جهة لقضية مخصوصة، ليس المحمول فيها الوجود نفسه، فليس هذا الإمكان إمكان الوجود كما سيصرح به المصنف في المرصد الثالث في الوجوب، والإمكان والامتناع، ولئن شئت فتأمل في قولك زيد يصح أن يتصف بالعمى، وبهذا يندفع أيضاً بيان الدور بأن الإمكان قد أخذ في كل من تعريفي الموجود والمعدوم وهو عبارة عن سلب الضرورة عن طرفي الوجود والعدم وذلك لأن الإمكان في تعريف الموجود سلب ضرورة عدم المعلومية، والإخبار عن الموصول وفي تعريف المعدوم بمعنى سلب ذلك السلب ولا احتياج في شيء من التعريفين إلى نسبته إلى الوجود والعدم بل إلى الاتصاف تأمل .
The First Observatory - The Second Goal: On it being common. And the passive is something in which there is an effect from another, and the eternal is something that has no beginning, and the originated here is something that has a beginning. So it is not correct to take any of them in the definition of the existent. And the soundness of knowledge and information is the possibility of their existence, so defining by them is also cyclical. His saying: (existent with no beginning) for the non-existent that has no beginning is called eternal. His saying: (here) he only said here because 'originated' may be used in the sense of 'renewed' and thus includes the non-existent that has a beginning. His saying: (and the soundness of knowledge and information, etc.) its meaning is the possibility of knowledge and information, and possibility does not relate to something except by considering its existence in itself or its existence for another, so its meaning is the possibility of their existence. His saying: (and the soundness of knowledge and information, the possibility of their existence) there is discussion in it because possibility in your saying 'it is possible to know and inform about it' is a modality of a specific proposition, whose predicate is not existence itself. So this possibility is not the possibility of existence as the author will clarify in the Third Observatory on obligation, possibility and impossibility. And if you wish, reflect on your saying 'Zaid is valid to be described with blindness.' And by this, the demonstration of circularity is also refuted: that possibility has been taken in each of the definitions of the existent and the non-existent, and it is the negation of necessity regarding both sides of existence and non-existence. That is because the possibility in the definition of the existent is the negation of the necessity of non-knowability and informing about the relative clause, and in the definition of the non-existent it means the negation of that negation. And there is no need in either definition to relate it to existence or non-existence, but rather to description. Reflect.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage refutes several proposed definitions of 'the existent' (al-mawjud). It begins by criticizing definitions using attributes like 'passive' (al-munfa'il), 'eternal' (al-qadim), and 'originated' (al-hadith) because these are not universal: the passive requires an effect from another, the eternal has no beginning, the originated has a beginning, so they do not apply to everything. Similarly, defining the existent as 'that which it is sound to know and inform about' leads to a cycle (dawr), because the soundness of knowledge and information presupposes possibility (imkan), and possibility itself is defined in terms of existence and non-existence. The commentary explains each term: 'existent with no beginning' refers to the eternal, but the non-existent can also be described as eternal; 'here' specifies that 'originated' is used in the sense of 'having a beginning' as opposed to 'renewed'. It then analyzes the cyclical argument in detail: the possibility taken in the definition is not the same as ontological possibility; rather it is a modality of a proposition. The defect is that possibility in both definitions ends up being the same, not related to existence itself. Thus the definitions fail.

Key Terms
الموجود
المنفعل
القديم
الحادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that attributes like 'passive' or 'eternal' are general enough to define the existent.
Correction: These attributes are not universal: passive excludes the First Cause, eternal excludes contingent beings, and originated excludes God. A correct definition must apply to every existent.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject using 'passive' (al-munfa'il) in the definition of the existent?
A) Because it contradicts the Quran.
B) Because not every existent receives an effect from another.
C) Because it leads to an infinite regress.
D) Because it is synonymous with 'originated'.

Key Takeaway
A valid definition of the existent must be universal—it cannot rely on attributes that are not true of every existing thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define God using attributes like 'Eternal', do you ever fall into the trap of thinking that such attributes also define all of existence? How will you now guard against making this mistake in your own understanding?
?
What does this lesson reveal about your own tendency to define things too narrowly? Can you think of a time when you defined someone or something by a single attribute, only to realize you excluded essential aspects? How might you change your approach now?
?
This lesson forces you to accept that no single attribute can capture the entirety of the existent. Does that unsettle you? Are you comfortable with the limitations of human definitions when it comes to understanding God and creation? What does that mean for your faith?

Circularity in Defining the Existent by Knowledge and Information
Refutes definition by soundness of knowledge as circular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining 'the existent' as 'that which it is sound to know and inform about' leads to a vicious circle.

Core Idea
Defining 'existent' by possibility of knowledge presupposes the very existence it tries to define.

The second refutation targets a more subtle definition: 'the existent is that of which knowledge and information are sound'. The author shows that this definition is cyclical because 'soundness of knowledge and information' reduces to 'possibility of their existence', which itself depends on the concept of existence. The commentary explains that the possibility here is not the same as ontological possibility, but the cycle remains if not careful.

Guided Path
1
Read the second refutation
Read the passage starting from 'وصحة العلم والإخبار' to the end. Note the claim that defining by soundness of knowledge is cyclical.
The passage includes commentary that elaborates on the cycle. Pay attention to the analysis of 'imkan'.
2
Understand the cycle
The definition says: existent = that which it is possible to know/inform about. But 'possibility of knowledge' means 'possibility of the existence of knowledge'. This 'possibility of existence' already assumes the concept of existence, so the definition is circular.
The circle: defining existence by possibility of knowledge, which itself uses existence.
3
Analyze the discussion on 'imkan'
The commentary points out that the possibility in 'it is possible to know' is a modal predicate, not the same as existential possibility. However, the cycle is still present because the definition implicitly uses existence.
This nuance shows the depth of the argument. The author offers a way to refute the cycle by analyzing the meaning of 'imkan' in different contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثاني : في أنه مشترك
والمنفعل موجود فيه أثر من الغير والقديم موجود لا أول له والحادث هاهنا موجود له أول، فلا يصح أخذ شيء منها في تعريف الموجود، وصحة العلم والإخبار إمكان وجودهما فالتعريف بها أيضا دوري.

قوله : ( موجود لا أول له ) فإن المعدوم الذي لا أول له يقال له أزلي . قوله : (هاهنا ) إنما قال هاهنا لأنه قد يطلق الحادث بمعنى المتجدد فيشمل المعدوم الذي له أول .
قوله : ( وصحة العلم والإخبار (الخ ) فإن معناها إمكان العلم والإخبار والإمكان لا يتعلق بشيء لا باعتبار وجوده في نفسه، أو وجوده لغيره فيكون معناه إمكان وجودهما .
قوله : ( وصحة العلم والإخبار إمكان وجودهما فيه بحث لأن الإمكان في قولك يمكن أن يعلم ويخبر عنه جهة لقضية مخصوصة، ليس المحمول فيها الوجود نفسه، فليس هذا الإمكان إمكان الوجود كما سيصرح به المصنف في المرصد الثالث في الوجوب، والإمكان والامتناع، ولئن شئت فتأمل في قولك زيد يصح أن يتصف بالعمى، وبهذا يندفع أيضاً بيان الدور بأن الإمكان قد أخذ في كل من تعريفي الموجود والمعدوم وهو عبارة عن سلب الضرورة عن طرفي الوجود والعدم وذلك لأن الإمكان في تعريف الموجود سلب ضرورة عدم المعلومية، والإخبار عن الموصول وفي تعريف المعدوم بمعنى سلب ذلك السلب ولا احتياج في شيء من التعريفين إلى نسبته إلى الوجود والعدم بل إلى الاتصاف تأمل .
The First Observatory - The Second Goal: On it being common. And the passive is something in which there is an effect from another, and the eternal is something that has no beginning, and the originated here is something that has a beginning. So it is not correct to take any of them in the definition of the existent. And the soundness of knowledge and information is the possibility of their existence, so defining by them is also cyclical. His saying: (existent with no beginning) for the non-existent that has no beginning is called eternal. His saying: (here) he only said here because 'originated' may be used in the sense of 'renewed' and thus includes the non-existent that has a beginning. His saying: (and the soundness of knowledge and information, etc.) its meaning is the possibility of knowledge and information, and possibility does not relate to something except by considering its existence in itself or its existence for another, so its meaning is the possibility of their existence. His saying: (and the soundness of knowledge and information, the possibility of their existence) there is discussion in it because possibility in your saying 'it is possible to know and inform about it' is a modality of a specific proposition, whose predicate is not existence itself. So this possibility is not the possibility of existence as the author will clarify in the Third Observatory on obligation, possibility and impossibility. And if you wish, reflect on your saying 'Zaid is valid to be described with blindness.' And by this, the demonstration of circularity is also refuted: that possibility has been taken in each of the definitions of the existent and the non-existent, and it is the negation of necessity regarding both sides of existence and non-existence. That is because the possibility in the definition of the existent is the negation of the necessity of non-knowability and informing about the relative clause, and in the definition of the non-existent it means the negation of that negation. And there is no need in either definition to relate it to existence or non-existence, but rather to description. Reflect.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage refutes several proposed definitions of 'the existent' (al-mawjud). It begins by criticizing definitions using attributes like 'passive' (al-munfa'il), 'eternal' (al-qadim), and 'originated' (al-hadith) because these are not universal: the passive requires an effect from another, the eternal has no beginning, the originated has a beginning, so they do not apply to everything. Similarly, defining the existent as 'that which it is sound to know and inform about' leads to a cycle (dawr), because the soundness of knowledge and information presupposes possibility (imkan), and possibility itself is defined in terms of existence and non-existence. The commentary explains each term: 'existent with no beginning' refers to the eternal, but the non-existent can also be described as eternal; 'here' specifies that 'originated' is used in the sense of 'having a beginning' as opposed to 'renewed'. It then analyzes the cyclical argument in detail: the possibility taken in the definition is not the same as ontological possibility; rather it is a modality of a proposition. The defect is that possibility in both definitions ends up being the same, not related to existence itself. Thus the definitions fail.

Key Terms
صحة العلم والإخبار
إمكان
دوري
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'soundness of knowledge and information' is a clear and non-circular definition of existence.
Correction: The definition is circular because 'soundness' reduces to 'possibility of existence', which assumes the concept of existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the definition 'the existent is that of which knowledge and information are sound' to be cyclical?
A) Because it uses the word 'existent' in the definition.
B) Because 'soundness of knowledge' means 'possibility of the existence of knowledge', which already assumes existence.
C) Because it leads to an infinite regress.
D) Because it contradicts the definition of the non-existent.

Key Takeaway
Defining the existent by reference to knowledge or information leads to circularity because the definition smuggles in the very notion of existence it aims to define.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to affirm the existence of something in your life—like God, truth, or even your own purpose—do you ever catch yourself assuming it must be knowable to be real? How does that assumption secretly make your reasoning circular, and what would it take to break that cycle?
?
Think of a belief you hold about the world—anything from the reality of other minds to the existence of free will. If you define that belief by your ability to know it, are you actually proving its existence or just assuming it? How does that realization unsettle your certainty?
?
You define yourself by what others can know about you—your actions, words, reputation. But if your identity is reduced to what is knowable, isn't that a circular trap? What would it mean to let go of needing to be defined at all?

The Self-Evident Nature of Existence's Commonality
Al-Jurjani argues that the commonality of existence is self-evident and refutes objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proposition that existence is common in meaning is considered self-evident and how objections to this are refuted.

Core Idea
Existence is common in meaning — this is self-evident, only the obstinate deny it.

This passage defends the claim that existence (wujud) is common in meaning across all existing things. The author insists that this is self-evident (daruri) and requires only minimal prompting, not a full proof. He then addresses several objections, including the worry that if it were self-evident, no proof would be needed, and the objection that the proof offered is circular. The discussion culminates in a detailed analysis of particular existence and non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
The passage mixes original text with commentary; focus on the logical flow.
2
Identify the main claim and simple proof
Notice that the proposition 'existence is common in meaning' is said to be necessary. The proof is the observable sharing of existence between any two existents versus its absence between existent and non-existent.
The proof is not a formal demonstration but a reminder.
3
Examine the objections and responses
List the objections: (1) If self-evident, why provide proofs? (2) The proof seems circular. (3) The opponent denies knowledge of commonality. (4) The problem of particular existences. Understand how the author refutes each.
4
Analyze the argument about particular existence
Focus on the long argument that the exhaustive division between particular existence and its negation is rational, and the dependence on absolute existence.
This section is more technical but crucial for the defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه القضية ) أي كون الوجود مشتركاً معنى ( ضرورية ) لا حاجة فيها إلى دليل، بل يكفيها أدنى تنبيه ( إذ نعلم بالضرورة أن بين الموجود والموجود ) كالسواد والبياض الموجودين مثلاً ) من الشركة في الكون في الأعيان ما ليس بين الموجود والمعدوم) كالبياض والعنقاء وليس هذه الشركة في الكون المذكور، بحسب اتحاد الاسم لأنها ثابتة مع قطع النظر عن الألفاظ وأوضاعها ( وهذا ) الذي ذكرناه ( لا يمنعه إلا المعاند ) فإنه غير مقنع له، وأما بالنسبة إلى المنصف فهو قاطع فيما ادعيناه، كذا في المباحث المشرقية قال المصنف : (وتعود قضية الماهية والتشخص فإن الحال فيهما أيضاً ثبوتها فاندفع ما يتوهم من أن كون هذه القضية بديهية مناف للاستدلال ببداهتها عليها فالصواب أن يترك قوله الوجه الرابع ويقال : وقال بعض الفضلاء ليكون عديلاً لما فهم عن الوجوه السابقة من كون هذه القضية نظرية

قوله : (لا حاجة فيها إلى دليل إلخ فلا يرد أنها لو كانت ضرورية لما استدل عليها القوم لأنها تنبيهات عليها .

قوله : ( إذ نعلم إلخ ) دليل على الحكم بالبداهة فإنه قد يكون نظرياً .

قوله : أن بين الموجود إلخ) استدلال باشتراك الكون بين أي موجودين فرضنا وعدم اشتراكه بين الموجود والمعدوم على اشتراكه بين جميع الموجودات فلا يتوهمن أن الدليل عين المدعي .

قوله : ( فإنه غير مقنع له إذ له أن ينكر العلم بالأمر المشترك بين الموجودين.

الأوليات، ثم إن خصوص العدم ليس إلا بخصوص المضاف إليه وهو الوجود، الخاص فحينئذ لا نسلم أنه إذا وجد زيد بوجود آخر أو عدم بعدم آخر صدق أنه ليس موجوداً بوجوده الخاص، وكذب أنه معدوم بعدمه الخاص . غاية ما في الباب أنه لزم من هذا المحال المفروض أن يعدم زيد بعدم خاص وهو سلب وجوده الخاص ويوجد بوجود خاص غير ما أضيف إليه هذا العدم، أو يعدم بعدمين خاصين وبالجملة لا شك على تقدير تعدد الوجودات الخاصة، والعدمات الخاصة أن لكل وجود جزئي سلباً متعلقاً به، فحاصل الجواب أن الحصر بين الوجود الجزئي، وسلبه الذي يصدق عليه العدم الخاص عقلي، وهذا كلام حق لا غبار عليه لا يقال : الحصر الذي ادعى عقليته هو الذي أحد طرفيه كون الشيء ليس له وجود البتة، فالحصر العقلي فيما يكون أحد طرفيه العدم الخاص بمعزل عما فيه المستدل لأنا نقول : فالخصم لا يسلم تحقق الحصر العقلي حينئذ كيف وتحققه عقلي فيما يكون أحد طرفيه ما ذكرته موقوف على ثبوت الوجود المطلق فمن لا يسلمه لا يسلمه، نعم المتفق عليه تحققه بين الموجود والمعدوم وأما أن العدم فيه مفهوم واحد فهو مستدل عليه بعدم التمايز بين الأعدام، ولذا أجيب عنه بثبوت التمايز بالإضافة إلى الوجودات فليتأمل فيه حق التأمل. قوله : ( وتعود قضية الماهية والتشخص وأيضاً دعوى الضرورة في محل النزاع لا تسمع.
This proposition (i.e., that existence is common in meaning) is necessary; there is no need for a proof, rather the slightest prompting suffices. For we know by necessity that between an existent and an existent (such as blackness and whiteness, both existing) there is a sharing in existence in reals that is not between an existent and a non-existent (such as whiteness and the phoenix). And this sharing in the mentioned existence is not due to unity of name, because it is established while disregarding words and their conventions. This that we have mentioned is not prevented except by the obstinate, for it is not convincing to him, but for the fair-minded it is conclusive regarding what we claim. Thus in the Eastern Investigations, the author said: 'And the issue of quiddity and individuation returns, for the case concerning them is also established, so what is imagined that this proposition being self-evident conflicts with inferring from its self-evidence is repelled; the correct thing is to leave his saying the fourth face and to say: And some virtuous ones said, to be a counterpart to what was understood from the previous faces that this proposition is theoretical.' His saying: 'There is no need for a proof, etc.' is not rebutted that if it were necessary, the people would not have inferred from it, because they are reminders of it. His saying: 'For we know, etc.' is a proof for the judgment of self-evidence, though it may be theoretical. His saying: 'That between an existent, etc.' is an inference from the sharing of existence between any two existents we suppose and its non-sharing between existent and non-existent to its sharing among all existents, so let no one imagine that the proof is the same as the claim. His saying: 'He is not convincing to him' because he can deny the knowledge of the common thing between the two existents. The first principles, then the particularity of non-existence is only by the particularity of that to which it is attributed, i.e., particular existence. Thus we do not grant that if Zayd exists by another existence or is non-existent by another non-existence, it is true that he is not existent by his particular existence, and false that he is non-existent by his particular non-existence. The upshot of the matter is that from this supposed impossibility, Zayd would be non-existent by a particular non-existence which is the negation of his particular existence, and existent by a particular existence other than that to which this non-existence is attributed, or non-existent by two particular non-existences. In sum, there is no doubt on the assumption of multiple particular existences and particular non-existences that every particular existence has a negation belonging to it. The conclusion of the answer is that the exhaustive division between particular existence and its negation, which is called particular non-existence, is rational. This is true speech with no obscurity. It cannot be said: The exhaustive division whose rationality is claimed is that whose one side is the thing having no existence at all; so the rational exhaustive division where one side is particular non-existence is remote from what the disputant is in. For we say: The opponent does not grant the realization of the rational exhaustive division then. How? Its rational realization when one side is what you mentioned is dependent on the establishment of absolute existence, so whoever does not affirm it does not affirm it. Yes, what is agreed upon is its realization between existent and non-existent. As for non-existence being a single concept, it is inferred from the lack of differentiation between non-existences. Hence it was answered by the establishment of differentiation in relation to existences; let one reflect on it truly. His saying: 'And the issue of quiddity and individuation returns, and also the claim of necessity in the locus of disagreement is not heard.'
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the claim that the proposition 'existence is common in meaning' is self-evident (daruriyya). The commentator begins by stating that the proposition requires no proof, only minimal prompting. The evidence for its self-evidence is that we intuitively recognize a sharing of existence between any two existing things (e.g., blackness and whiteness) that is absent between an existing thing and a non-existing thing (e.g., whiteness and the phoenix). This sharing is not merely verbal but conceptual, independent of language. The only person who denies this is one who is obstinate, and the argument is conclusive for the fair-minded. The commentator then addresses several objections: (1) If the proposition were self-evident, why did scholars provide proofs? The answer is that those proofs are merely reminders, not demonstrations. (2) The proof that between existents there is sharing is itself a theoretical argument; the commentator notes that even a judgment of self-evidence can be supported by a theoretical argument. (3) Some might think that the inference from sharing between any two existents to sharing among all is circular; the commentator clarifies that the proof is not identical to the claim. (4) A detailed objection about the possibility of multiple particular existences and non-existences is answered by affirming that the exhaustive division between particular existence and its negation (particular non-existence) is rational, and that the opponent's denial of this is based on a refusal to accept absolute existence. The passage ends with a remark that the issue of quiddity and individuation is connected, but the claim of necessity is not accepted in the locus of disagreement.

مشترك

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a self-evident proposition cannot have any proof, but the author shows that reminders can be given.
Correction: Self-evident truths can be supported by reminders or prompting; the proof is not a demonstration but a clarification.

Key Takeaway
The commonality of existence is a self-evident truth that only an obstinate person would deny.

The Fifth Argument: Denial of Commonality Implies Commonality
Al-Jurjani presents a fifth argument that denying commonality of existence implicitly affirms it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the claim 'existence is not common' itself presupposes a single common concept of existence, making the denial self-refuting.

Core Idea
To deny commonality, you must already have a common concept in mind.

This lesson presents a clever logical argument from al-Jurjani's commentary: the very act of stating that existence is not common is a universal claim that requires a shared concept. The argument shows that the opponent's position is self-defeating. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
First, read the Arabic passage and the English translation carefully. Focus on the flow of the argument from the opening claim to the conclusion.
Notice that the passage includes both the argument and the commentator's explanation.
2
Identify the Core Paradox
The argument states that denying commonality requires a common concept. Identify why this is considered a paradox: the opponent's own statement works against him.
The opponent claims existence is not common, but his claim is universal.
3
Understand Why a Universal Claim Needs a Common Meaning
The passage explains that a universal claim covering multiple things can only be proven by a single proof if the claim is general, and generality comes from a single common concept. Grasp this logical step.
If the claim were not general, separate proofs would be needed.
4
See the Commentator's Clarification
The commentator clarifies that the subject of judgment is the single concept in relation to its individuals, not the individuals directly. This avoids a potential objection.
This distinction is subtle but important for the argument's validity.
5
Reflect on the Warning
The commentary ends with a warning not to misunderstand the opponent's position. The opponent is not denying commonality of particular existences, but rather that no existence at all is common. Yet even this denial requires a common concept.
This shows the depth of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس قال ذلك البعض من الفضلاء ) من ( زعم أنه يعني الوجود ( غير مشترك فقد اعترف بأنه مشترك من حيث لا يدري إذ لولا أنه تصور مفهوماً واحداً شاملاً لجميع الموجودات ( يحكم عليه بأنه غير مشترك ) بين الموجودات (للزمه البرهان في كل وجود أنه كذلك ) أي غير مشترك ( وإذا لم تكن الدعوى ( المتعلقة بأمور متعددة واحدة ( عامة) لها لم يمكن إثباتها بدليل واحد ( عام ) لأن تلك الدعوى حينئذ متعددة بحسب المعنى كتعدد تلك الأمور، فلا بد لكل واحدة من تلك الدعاوي من برهان على حدة، والحاصل أن الدليل إذا كان واحداً متناولاً لمتعدد، فلا بد أن تكون الدعوى عامة متناولة لذلك المتعدد وعمومها إياه إنما يكون بأخذ معنى واحد عام لجميعه، إذ لولاه لوجب التعرض لخصوصية كل واحد من ذلك المتعدد فمن قال : إن الوجود غير مشترك فلا شك أن حكمه هذا غير مقتصر على وجود واحد بل يتناول كل وجود فلو كان

قوله : ( الوجه الخامس قال إلخ) تقريره أنه لو لم يكن الوجود مشتركاً معنى لكان الحكم بأنه غير مشترك مطابقاً للواقع والتالي باطل لأن الحكم بأنه غير مشترك يستلزم الاشتراك، فلا يكون مطابقاً للواقع. قوله : ( يحكم عليه أي على ذلك المفهوم الواحد من حيث اتحاده بأفراده أعني الوجودات، فلا يرد عليه أن المحكوم عليه هي الأفراد لا العنوان فالصواب أن يقال : يحكم بملاحظته على تلك الوجودات .

قوله : ( وإذا لم تكن الظاهر لأنه إذا لم تكن لأنه دليل للملازمة المستفادة من الشرطية السابقة إلا أنه أورده بالعطف إشارة إلى أن هذه المقدمة محققة مقررة لا شبهة فيها مع قطع النظر عن جعلها دليل الملازمة . قوله : ( عامة لها بأن لا يوجد مفهوم مشترك بينها يجعل عنواناً لملاحظتها. قوله : ( لأن تلك الدعوى حينئذ أي حين فرض أن لا يوجد مفهوم شامل لتلك الأمور متعددة بحسب تعدد تلك الأمور، فكانت قضايا متعددة لا بد في الاستدلال عليها ملاحظة من كل واحد من تلك الأمور، بخصوصه وجعله أصغر وإثبات الأوسط له فيحصل صغريات متعددة تتعدد الدلائل بحسب تعددها مثلا إذا قيل : الوجودات نفس الحقائق ولا شيء من الحقائق بمشتركة وفرض أنه ليس مفهوماً واحداً يجعل آلة لملاحظة تلك الوجودات لا بد من ملاحظة كل واحد منها بخصوصه، ويقال : هذه حقيقة وتلك حقيقة فيحصل صغريات متكثرة حسب تكثر الوجودات، فضم إلى تلك الكبرى فلا يكون الدليل واحداً.
قوله : (بل يتناول إلخ لا يتوهم من هذا أن من قال : بأن الوجود غير مشترك أراد به أن الوجودات الخاصة غير مشتركة لأنه لا يقبل النزاع بل أراد أن لا شيء من الوجود بمشترك، ويلزم من هذا كون كل وجود خاصاً وغير مشترك فتدبر، فإنه زل فيه أقدام .
The fifth face: That some of the virtuous said: Whoever claims that existence is not common has admitted that it is common without realizing it, because if he had not conceived a single concept encompassing all existents, he would not have judged it to be not common among existents. For if the claim concerning multiple things is not general, it cannot be proven by a single general proof, because that claim would then be multiple in meaning according to the multiplicity of those things, so each of those claims would require a separate proof. The conclusion is that if a proof is one and covers multiple things, the claim must be general and cover that multiplicity, and its generality over them is only by taking a single common meaning for all of them, for otherwise one would have to attend to the particularity of each of those multiples. So whoever says that existence is not common, his judgment is undoubtedly not restricted to one existence but encompasses every existence; if it were... His saying: (The fifth face, he said, etc.) Its purport is that if existence were not common in meaning, then the judgment that it is not common would correspond to reality, but the consequent is false because the judgment that it is not common entails commonality, so it would not correspond to reality. His saying: (He judges it) meaning that single concept in terms of its unity with its individuals, i.e., existences, so it is not objected that the subject of judgment is the individuals, not the concept; rather the correct thing is to say: He judges by attending to it upon those existences. His saying: (And if it is not) the apparent meaning is 'because if it is not' as a proof for the implication derived from the preceding conditional, except that he presented it with a conjunction to indicate that this premise is established and determined with no doubt, regardless of making it a proof for the implication. His saying: (General for them) meaning that there is no common concept between them that could be made a title for attending to them. His saying: (Because that claim then) meaning when it is assumed that there is no inclusive concept for those matters, it becomes multiple according to the multiplicity of those matters, so they are multiple propositions; in inferring them, it is necessary to attend to each one of those matters individually, to make it the minor term, and to establish the middle term for it, resulting in multiple minor premises, and the proofs become multiple according to their multiplicity. For example, if it is said: 'Existences are the same as realities, and none of the realities are common,' and it is assumed that there is no single concept to use as an instrument for attending to those existences, then one must attend to each one individually, saying: 'This is a reality and that is a reality,' thus obtaining numerous minor premises according to the multiplicity of existences, then adding the major premise; so the proof is not one. His saying: (Rather it encompasses, etc.) Do not imagine from this that whoever says existence is not common means that particular existences are not common, because this is not debatable; rather he means that nothing of existence is common, and from this it follows that every existence is particular and not common. So reflect, for feet have slipped on this.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument for the commonality of existence, attributed to some virtuous scholar, along with the commentator's explanation. The argument is: If someone claims that existence is not common (i.e., not a single concept shared by all existents), he must already have a single concept of existence in mind to make that claim. Otherwise, his claim would not be a single judgment covering all existents, and he would need separate proofs for each existence. The commentator explains the logic: The judgment 'existence is not common' is a single universal judgment; if existence were not a single concept, the judgment would not be universal. The commentator then clarifies that the subject of the judgment is the concept, not the individuals, and that the opponent's position is self-defeating because to deny commonality, one must use a common concept. The commentator also addresses the objection that the argument assumes a single concept when the opponent might deny that. He explains that the opponent's own claim of a universal statement implies the existence of a common concept. The passage concludes with a warning not to misunderstand: the opponent does not deny that particular existences are common (that is obvious), but that no existence at all is common; yet this very denial requires a common concept.

Key Terms
الوجه الخامس
مشترك
مفهوم واحد
دعوى عامة
برهان واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Some might think the opponent's position is that particular existences are not common (which is obvious and not debated).
Correction: The opponent actually means that no existence at all is common (i.e., existence itself is not a shared concept). But this very universal denial requires a common concept, making the position self-defeating.

Key Takeaway
The universal judgment 'existence is not common' logically entails that existence is a single common concept, making the denial self-refuting.
