The First Observation - The Fourth Objective: On Mental Existence. (A branch of its establishment) i.e., the establishment of that other (in itself, and since it is not) the establishment of those conceived things (in external existence, so it is in the mind, and that is what is sought). For if you say: If what you have mentioned is correct—that the subject of true positive judgments must be existent, either externally or mentally—then our statement would be true: 'The absolute non-existent,' which has no existence at all, neither externally nor mentally, 'is not known nor spoken of,' because its being known and spoken of in reality necessitates its existence in some sense, and since it has no existence at all, there is no knowledge nor information. And it is a contradiction because the absolute non-existent becomes subject to the attribute of lacking knowledge and information about it, so it is absolutely non-existent and existent in some sense. We say: The necessary consequence of what we mentioned is that what you have stated is true as a negative proposition, meaning that it is not an absolute non-existent that is known and spoken of. And a true negative proposition does not require the existence of the subject; rather, what requires it is a true affirmative proposition, so there is no contradiction. It is not true in the sense that there exists a thing of which it is true in reality that it is an absolute non-existent and its attribute is that it is not known nor spoken of, such that it would be an affirmative modified proposition requiring the existence of the subject. His statement: 'Since the establishment of a thing, etc.' means that a true judgment requires the establishment of that predicate for it in reality, and the establishment of a thing for a thing in reality necessitates the establishment of that to which it is attributed. His statement: 'It became subject to the attribute of lacking knowledge'—he did not say 'subject to lacking knowledge' lest it be objected that the discussion is about positive matters, and lacking knowledge and information is not positive, unlike the attribute of it, for it is a positive concept whose object is non-existent. His statement: 'So it is absolutely non-existent and existent in some sense'—he did not say 'so the absolute non-existent becomes external' because there is no establishment for the mind at the time of the establishment of the predicate to the subject such that the necessary establishment would be mental. The answer, after granting the necessity of impossible things being characterized by impossibility in every case, is that if the non-existence of the higher principles is included in this supposition, then we may not grant that impossibles are characterized by impossibility, on the basis that whether the impossibility is essential or otherwise, it is possible that it necessitates the impossible, as is well-known. And if it is not included, then the establishment of the subject in external existence is not necessary, because it is possible that its establishment is in one of those principles by a shadow existence, since the purpose here is to prove a kind of distinction for intelligibles other than distinction by external existence, whether the mind invents them or observes them from a subject, as we will mention. In short, it is known for certain that the characterization of impossibles by impossibility is not by the consideration of the considerer or the supposition of the supposer. As for their characterization by it on the assumption of no perceiving power at all, the opponent who claims that the establishment of a thing for a thing is a branch of the establishment of that to which it is attributed does not concede it. And the claim of necessity in the place of dispute, especially in a judgment on which the majority of rational people have agreed to the contrary, is not heeded. By this, the refutation of what the professor, the investigator, has objected becomes clear: that we know for certain that the non-existents which have existence in the mind—if mental existence is granted—their possibility of existence in it, i.e., the equality of their existence and non-existence in it with respect to their essences, is established before their existence in the mind. So their existence before its realization in any way is characterized in reality by its equality to non-existence. And even if we grant that existence is existent, if it is characterized in reality by its equality to non-existence, then non-existence would necessarily be characterized in it by its equality to existence; otherwise, one of the two real relatives would be realized without the other, and this is false by necessity and agreement, especially since this non-existence has no existence at all. His statement: 'And existent in some sense' i.e., by virtue of being attributed to lacking knowledge and information about it, not by virtue of ... His statement: 'We say: the necessary consequence of what we mentioned, etc.' It is not hidden that what he mentioned is our statement: 'Everyone who is subject to a true positive judgment must be absolutely existent,' and this converts by contrapositive to our statement: 'Everything that is not absolutely existent, i.e., everything that is absolutely non-existent, is not subject to a true positive judgment,' such that it would be an affirmative modified proposition of both sides because the converse of a universal affirmative on the method of the ancients is a universal affirmative. Perhaps he based the answer on the method of the later scholars, which is that the converse of a universal affirmative is a universal negative composed of the opposite of the predicate and the same subject, as he explained by his statement: 'It is true as a negative, meaning that it is not an absolute non-existent that is known and spoken of.' His statement: 'It does not require the existence of the subject' which is the basis for the truth of affirmation, although it requires the conception of the subject, and this does not necessitate the establishment of mental existence for it. If mere conception were sufficient for that, then it would be sufficient in the proof to say: we conceive what has no existence externally, so it would be existent in the mind. His statement: 'Requiring the existence of the subject, according to what is verified. And if we say that it does not require existence, then the objection falls from its root.'
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the theory of mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni) and provides a detailed response. The objection argues that if mental existence is true, then the statement 'The absolute non-existent is not known nor spoken of' would be contradictory because it predicates something (being unknown) of a subject, requiring the subject to exist in some sense, but the absolute non-existent has no existence at all. The answer distinguishes between negative and affirmative judgments: the statement 'The absolute non-existent is not known' is a negative proposition, which does not require the existence of its subject. Only affirmative propositions require subject existence. The commentator then explains the nuances: the objector's phrasing avoids making the predicate itself negative; he speaks of 'being attributed to lack of knowledge' which is a positive concept (attribution) with a non-existent object. The response also discusses the principle 'the establishment of a thing for a thing is a branch of the establishment of that to which it is attributed' and applies it to impossibles. The long commentary on the answer shows that the answer is based on the contrapositive of the original claim, converting a universal affirmative to a universal negative, which does not require subject existence. It also emphasizes that mere conception of a thing does not entail its mental existence, otherwise any imagined thing would exist mentally.

Key Terms
ثبوت الشيء لشيء
عكس النقيض
الممتنع
القضية الموجبة الكلية
القضية السالبة الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Contrapositive preserves the same type of proposition (affirmative/negative).
Correction: Contrapositive of a universal affirmative is a universal negative when using the method of later scholars (as Al-Jurjani does).

Checkpoint
What is the logical form of the contrapositive of 'Every subject of a true positive judgment must be existent' according to Al-Jurjani's answer?
A) A universal affirmative: 'Every existent is subject of a true positive judgment.'
B) A universal negative: 'Everything not absolutely existent is not subject to a true positive judgment.'
C) A particular negative: 'Some non-existent is not subject of a true positive judgment.'
D) A particular affirmative: 'Some existent is subject of a true positive judgment.'

Key Takeaway
The contrapositive transforms the universal affirmative into a universal negative, which does not require subject existence, thereby reinforcing the answer that the objection is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You encounter a theological claim that seems impossible by your measure. Do you immediately dismiss it, or do you pause to consider that your measure of possibility might be incomplete? This lesson shows that even impossibles can be characterized in the mind. When was the last time you rejected a truth because it did not fit your narrow concept of existence?
?
Al-Jurjani uses logical precision to defend a position. Where in your own faith are you relying on vague reasoning instead of disciplined thought? Will you commit today to studying the logical foundations of your beliefs, even when they challenge your comfort?
?
The objection against al-Jurjani seemed strong, but a subtle shift (using contrapositive as a universal negative) refuted it. Is there a belief you hold that you have never scrutinized because you assume it is obviously true? What would happen if you subjected it to the same logical rigor—could it withstand the test?

Absolute Non-Existent as Opposite: Objection and Answer
Al-Jurjani presents and answers a second objection about the absolute non-existent being opposite to the absolute existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the absolute non-existent can be opposite to the absolute existent without implying its own existence, through the distinction between concept and subject of judgment.

Core Idea
A concept can be opposite without the thing itself existing.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: if the absolute non-existent is nothing, how can it be opposite to existence? Al-Jurjani answers by distinguishing between the concept of the non-existent and its status as a subject of judgment. The commentary further clarifies the nature of predicates and propositions, showing that the objection mistakenly treats a conceptual relationship as requiring existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the structure: objection, answer, and two comments (قوله).
2
Identify the objection
The objector says: If your previous answer were correct, then the statement 'The absolute non-existent is opposite to the absolute existent' would be false. Locate this in the text.
The objection hinges on the concept of 'absolute non-existent' being opposite.
3
Find the core answer
Al-Jurjani answers by distinguishing between the concept (مفهوم) of the absolute non-existent and it being a subject of judgment. The concept itself is opposite, but the non-existent is not a subject requiring existence.
This is the key distinction: conceptual opposition does not entail subject existence.
4
Understand the commentary
The commentary explains the objector's reformulation and the nature of the predicate. It notes that the predicate is non-existential (like lack of knowledge), so it does not require subject existence even in an affirmative modified proposition.
This reinforces the answer by logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن عاد وقال : لو صح ما ذكرتم لما صدق قولنا : المعدوم المطلق مقابل للموجود المطلق قلنا : مفهوم المعدوم المطلق من حيث هو هو مقابل للموجود محكوماً عليه، وأن لا يكون محكوماً عليه كما قالوا في مسألة المجهول المطلق لأن الكلام هاهنا مسوق لنفي الوجود الذهني فالمناسب أن يقال : لو صح ما ذكرتم يلزم أن يكون معدوماً وموجوداً، بخلاف مسألة المجهول المطلق، فإنها مسوقة لنفي استدعاء كل تصديق للتصورات الثلاث.
قوله : ( فإن عاد إلخ ) أي عاد الناقض وحرر النقض باعتبار مفهوم المعدوم المطلق، وقال: الحكم، لأنه خروج عن السوق فالجواب تام لكن في تفريع السؤال عما قبله مناقشة ظاهرة، لأن المحمول فيما ذكر أمر عدمي لان المذكور فيما سبق أن الحكم بالمحمولات الثبوتية أعني التي لا يدخل السلب في مفهومها يستدعي أحد الوجودين فلا يصدق قوله لوصح هذا إلخ إلا بتعسف فتدبر وعدم العلم والإخبار عنه ليس بمفهوم ثبوتي حتى يقتضي وجود الموضوع، ويتحقق التناقض باعتباره اللهم إلا أن يعتبر المحمول الاتصاف بهما كما أشار إليه الشارح لكنه بعيد من عبارة المصنف، فليتأمل .
قوله : (حتى كون قضية موجبة معدولة إلخ) ليس معدولية القضية واقتضائها وجود الموضوع باعتبار حمل المعدوم المطلق على الأمر، حتى يقال : معنى معدوم مطلق مسلوب عنه الوجود المطلق، فيكون موجبة سالبة المحمول، وهي عندهم لا تقتضي أيضاً وجود الموضوع،
For if he returns and says: If what you have mentioned were correct, then our statement 'The absolute non-existent is opposite to the absolute existent' would not be true. We say: The concept of the absolute non-existent in itself is opposite to the existent as subject, and that it is not subject, as they said in the issue of the absolutely unknown, because the discussion here is aimed at negating mental existence, so it is appropriate to say: If what you have mentioned were correct, it would necessitate that it be non-existent and existent, unlike the issue of the absolutely unknown, for that is aimed at negating that every assent requires the three conceptions. His statement: 'If he returns, etc.' meaning the objector returned and formulated the objection in terms of the concept of the absolute non-existent, and said: 'The judgment,' because it is a departure from the flow, so the answer is complete, but there is an apparent objection in the derivation of the question from what preceded it, because the predicate in what was mentioned is a non-existential matter, since what was mentioned previously is that judgment by positive predicates—i.e., those in which negation does not enter into their concept—requires one of the two existences, so his statement 'If this were correct, etc.' is not true except with difficulty, so reflect. And lack of knowledge and information about it is not a positive concept such that it would require the existence of the subject and cause contradiction by virtue of it, unless the predicate is considered to be the attribute of both, as the commentator pointed out, but that is far from the author's expression, so consider. His statement: 'Such that it would be an affirmative modified proposition, etc.' The modification of the proposition and its requirement for the existence of the subject is not by virtue of predicating the absolute non-existent of the thing, such that it is said: 'The meaning of absolute non-existent is that from which absolute existence is negated,' so it would be an affirmative with a negated predicate, and they also do not require the existence of the subject.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection: if the absolute non-existent cannot be the subject of a true affirmative judgment (as argued in the first answer), then the proposition 'The absolute non-existent is opposite to the absolute existent' would be false. The answer distinguishes between the concept of the absolute non-existent and its being a subject of judgment. The concept itself is opposite to the existent, but that does not make the absolute non-existent an existing subject. The commentary on the objection notes that the objector refines his objection in terms of the concept, but the answer remains complete. The commentator also points out that the predicate in the objection is non-existential (lack of knowledge) and thus does not require subject existence. The final comment on 'affirmative modified proposition' clarifies that even if the proposition is considered affirmative but modified, it still does not require subject existence because the predicate is essentially negative.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That saying 'the absolute non-existent is opposite to the absolute existent' implies the non-existent has some reality or existence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is the concept of the non-existent that is opposite; the non-existent itself is not a subject of judgment and does not require existence. Conceptual opposition does not entail real opposition.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction the answer makes to refute the second objection?
A) The absolute non-existent has a mental existence that makes it opposite to the existent.
B) The concept of the absolute non-existent is opposite to the existent, but the non-existent itself is not a subject.
C) The proposition is false because the absolute non-existent cannot be opposite to anything.
D) The objection confuses mental and external existence.

Key Takeaway
The absolute non-existent can be opposite in concept without itself existing, because conceptual opposition does not require subject existence.

The Concept of Absolute Non-Existent in the Mind
Explanation of why the concept of absolute non-existent is both opposite to existent and an instance of it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of absolute non-existent can be both opposite to existent and an instance of mental existence.

Core Idea
Absolute non-existent exists as a mental concept without external reality.

This passage resolves a philosophical puzzle: how can we meaningfully speak of 'absolute non-existent' if it is nothing at all? The answer lies in distinguishing between the concept's meaning and its mental existence. Let's see how the commentator explains this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: it begins with a statement about 'absent existence' and then explains the relation.
2
Identify the two respects
The passage distinguishes between the concept in itself and its existence in the mind. Make sure you understand both.
3
Analyze the example
The example of 'affirmation' (tasdiq) and 'simple conception' (tasawwur) illustrates the principle. Reflect on how they are opposite yet one is an instance of the other.
4
Connect to the bigger picture
This argument defends the theory of mental existence against the objection that non-existent cannot be subject of predication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فله وجود غائب فلغيبوبته توهم أنه غير موجود.
قوله : ( مفهوم المعدوم إلخ) يعني لا منافاة بين كون مفهوم المعدوم المطلق مقابلاً لموجود المطلق وفرداً منه، فإنه من حيث هو مع قطع النظر عن وجوده في الذهن مقابل له، ومن حيث إنه متصور موجود في الذهن فرد منه ولا استحالة فيه، فإن مفهوم التصديق مقابل للتصور الساذج من حيث هو ومن حيث حصوله في الذهن تصور ساذج وأمثال ذلك كثير.
His saying: (So it has an absent existence, and due to its absence it is imagined to be non-existent.) His saying: (The concept of the non-existent, etc.) means there is no contradiction between the concept of the absolute non-existent being opposite to the absolute existent and being an individual of it. For it, in itself, apart from its existence in the mind, is opposite to it, and in terms of its being conceived and existing in the mind, it is an individual of it, and there is no impossibility in that. For the concept of affirmation (tasdiq) is opposite to simple conception (tasawwur) in itself, and in terms of its occurrence in the mind it is a simple conception, and many similar examples exist.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage resolves a paradox that arises from the philosophers' objection to mental existence. The objection holds that if we judge a term like 'absolute non-existent' as a genuine subject of predication, then it must have some reality, contradicting its non-existence. The answer distinguishes two respects: in itself, the concept of absolute non-existent is opposite to the concept of existent, since it signifies negation. But as a mental content, it exists in the mind and is thus an individual instance of the category 'existent in the mind'. The commentator illustrates with the example of 'affirmation' (tasdiq) being opposite to 'simple conception' (tasawwur) in definition, yet when it occurs in the mind it is itself a simple conception. Thus no contradiction: something can be opposite in meaning while existing in the same mental domain.

وجود ذهني
مقابل
فرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Absolute non-existent cannot exist in any way.
Correction: It exists as a mental concept without external reality, as explained in the passage.

Checkpoint

A) It is opposite in meaning but exists externally.
B) It is opposite in meaning but exists in the mind as a concept.
C) It is an individual of existent only when considered externally.
D) It is not really opposite; it is the same as existent.

Key Takeaway
The concept of absolute non-existent is opposite to existent in meaning but exists in the mind as a mental concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Two Theories of Mental Forms and Their Refutation
Refutation of two theories of mental forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two alternative theories of where mental forms originate (Platonic Forms and Active Intellect) and how the commentator refutes them by showing that neither places forms in the human mind.

Core Idea
Mental forms are not in the mind; they are witnessed by the soul from an external source.

This passage addresses a critical objection to mental existence: if forms cannot exist in the mind, perhaps they exist elsewhere—like in Plato's separate Forms or in the Active Intellect. The commentator examines both theories and shows that even if true, they do not prove that mental forms exist in the human mind; rather, they remain external objects of perception.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two theories
Identify the two proposed loci for mental forms: Platonic self-subsistent Forms (al-muthul) and the Active Intellect (al-'aql al-fa''al) in which forms are impressed.
The passage first mentions 'either self-subsistent or subsistent in another' referring to these two.
2
Examine the refutation
Notice the key point: when the soul turns to those forms (whether self-subsistent or in the Active Intellect), it witnesses them without them being in the mind. Thus, they are not mental existents.
The phrase 'when the soul turns to them' is central.
3
Appreciate the nuance on Plato's Forms
The commentator notes that interpreting Plato's Forms as self-subsistent luminous realities of God's knowledge is more effective for the denial, but it still does not make them mental.
This strengthens the refutation by showing even a strong interpretation fails.
4
Recognize the conclusion
Conclude that neither theory supports mental existence; forms remain external, perceived but not imprinted in the human mind.
The refutation works for both theories equally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إما قائم بنفسه إلخ) أي متردد بين هذين الأمرين لا أنه منقسم فكل واحد من الأمرين سند المنع. كما سيشير إليه في تحقيق الاستدلال الثالث على الوجود الذهني، بل باعتبار حمل ما لا يعلم ولا يخبر عنه على ذلك الأمر.
قوله : ( ولو حمل إلخ) يعني أن المذكور في الكتب حمل قول أفلاطون على المثل، وهو إن كان كافياً في تقوية المنع بناء على أنه إذا جاز وجود المثل المجردة للطبائع النوعية، فليجز مثلها في جميع المفهومات التي نتصورها لكن الحمل على أن صور معلومات الله تعالى قائمة بذواتها وأنه لا بعد في أن تكون الحقائق النورية قائمة بأنفسها في عالم الأنوار لكماليتها وتماميتها في أنفسها، وعدم قيامها في عالم الجسمانيات لكونها ناقصة وكمالاً لغيرها، كما جوزوا كون الشيء جوهراً أو عرضاً باعتبار الوجودين أنسب، فإنه لاستلزامه وجود كل ما نتصوره بالفعل أدخل في تقوية المنع من مجرد الجواز .
قوله : ( أن يرتسم فيه صور ما يوجده لأن إيجاده مسبوق بالعلم وليس على سبيل الطبع كالحرارة عن النار، والعلم عبارة عن الصورة المرتسمة في العاقل قوله : (ما يوجده) ولكون ما يوجده مشتملا على الأجزاء والعوارض الثبوتية والعدمية والإضافية الممكنة الوجود وممتنعه لا بد أن يكون صور جميعها مرتسمة فيه . قوله : (فإذا التفتت إلخ ) يعني إذا التفتت النفس إلى تلك الصور سواء كانت قائمة بنفسها أو بغيرها شاهدتها من غير أن تكون حاصلة فيها، فلا تكون موجودة في الذهن فهو متفرع على كلا التقديرين وليس مختصا بتقدير الارتسام وإن كان ظاهر العبارة توهمه .
His saying: (Either self-subsistent, etc.) i.e., it is undecided between these two matters, not that it is divided, so each of the two matters is a ground for denial. As he will indicate in the verification of the third proof for mental existence, but rather in consideration of what is not known or reported about that matter. His saying: (And if he had interpreted, etc.) means that what is mentioned in the books is interpreting Plato's statement as referring to the Ideas. While this is sufficient to strengthen the denial based on the fact that if the existence of abstract Ideas for specific natures is allowed, then similar Ideas for all concepts we conceive should be allowed. However, interpreting it as that the forms of God's knowledge are self-subsistent, and that it is not far-fetched for luminous realities to be self-subsistent in the world of lights due to their perfection and completeness in themselves, and not subsisting in the world of bodies because they are deficient and perfections for others, as they allow a thing to be a substance or accident depending on the two modes of existence, is more appropriate. For it entails the actual existence of everything we conceive, which is more effective in strengthening the denial than mere possibility. His saying: (That images of what He creates are impressed in it) because His creation is preceded by knowledge and not by nature like heat from fire, and knowledge is the image impressed in the intellect. His saying: (What He creates) and because what He creates includes parts and positive, negative, and relational accidents, both possible and impossible, it is necessary that images of all of them be impressed in it. His saying: (And when the soul turns to them, etc.) means when the soul turns to those images, whether they are self-subsistent or subsistent in another, it witnesses them without them being present in it, so they are not existent in the mind. This follows from both suppositions and is not specific to the supposition of impression, though the apparent wording might suggest it.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage discusses two alternative theories about the locus of the conceptual forms that we perceive. The first is Plato's theory of self-subsistent Forms (al-muthul), where each universal nature has an eternal, separate reality. The second is the philosophers' theory of the Active Intellect, where all forms are impressed as objects of divine knowledge and creativity. The commentator first notes that both theories are intended to refute the claim that mental existence is impossible by providing an external locus for forms. He then critiques them: if the forms are in the Active Intellect or are self-subsistent, when the soul turns to them, it directly witnesses them without them being in the human mind. Thus, these theories do not establish that the forms exist in the human mind; rather, they move the locus outside. The passage also notes that interpreting Plato's Forms as the self-subsistent luminous realities of God's knowledge is more effective, because it would imply that every conceivable form actually exists externally, which would make mental existence unnecessary. However, the main response is that in neither case do the forms have existence in the human mind; perceived forms are not 'in' the mind but are extra-mental realities witnessed by the soul.

Key Terms
قائم بنفسه
قائم بغيره
المثل
العقل الفعال
النفس
ارتسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if forms exist in the Active Intellect or as Platonic Forms, then they also exist in the human mind when perceived.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the soul merely witnesses these external forms; they do not become imprinted in the human mind. Thus, mental existence is not established.

Checkpoint
What is the main refutation of both the Platonic Forms and Active Intellect theories?
A) They are logically impossible.
B) The soul perceives them but they are not in the human mind.
C) They only apply to abstract concepts.
D) They require a separate world of lights.

Key Takeaway
Neither the Platonic Forms nor the Active Intellect can account for mental existence, because the soul only witnesses external forms without them being in the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What does this imply about your intellectual humility and dependence on a higher reality? Will you actively remind yourself that you are not the origin of truth?
?
If the forms you perceive are not actually in your mind but witnessed from a source beyond you, how does that change the way you seek knowledge? Are you turning your soul toward the true source with sincerity and need, or are you content with superficial impressions?
?
The passage refutes the idea that mental forms reside in the mind. Reflect on a time you were absolutely certain that an idea was 'your own.' Could it be that you were merely witnessing it from outside? How does this realization affect your accountability for pursuing truth and avoiding falsehood?
